Why Sanusi's Division Must Be the Third Type of Restriction
Al-Anbabi proves Sanusi's division is a third-type restriction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi's classification of rational judgments into three categories is not a proper logical division but a restriction (hasr) of the third type.

Core Idea
Sanusi's three judgments are not parts of a universal but a restriction of a subject.

In this passage, al-Anbabi clarifies a common misunderstanding about Sanusi's famous division. He shows that the three categories—wujub, istihala, jawaz—cannot be particulars or parts of the rational judgment. Instead, they are a restriction of the rational judgment to these three possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two types of proper division
Recall that a proper logical division is either dividing a universal into its particulars (e.g., animal into human, horse) or dividing a whole into its parts (e.g., house into roof, walls).
Sanusi's case must be tested against these two types.
2
See why it cannot be a universal-particular division
The text argues that we cannot say 'wujub is a rational judgment' because the rational judgment is defined as affirmation or negation, not as wujub itself. So wujub is not a particular of the universal 'rational judgment'.
This rejects the first type.
3
See why it cannot be a whole-part division
Similarly, wujub, istihala, and jawaz are not parts that compose the rational judgment. You cannot analyze the rational judgment into these three components.
This rejects the second type.
4
Recognize the third type of restriction
Since both proper types fail, the statement must be a restriction: it simply means that rational judgments do not go beyond these three categories. This is a different kind of hasr.
This is the core conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي.
(قوله: وَالثَّالِثُ حَصْرٌ بِمَعْنَى عَدَمِ الْخُرُوجِ) في جَعْلِه قسيماً لما قبله نَظَر، فالأولى أن يقال: إن الحصر معناه: عدم الخروج.
ثم إنه تارة يكون حَصرَ كلّي في جزئياته، وتارةً يكون حَصْرَ كُلِّ في أجزائه، وتارةً يكون حَصر متعلّق خاص - بالكسر - في متعلق خاص - بالفتح ، نحو: انحَصَرَتْ فِكْرَتي في ذنوبي، وانحصر حُكْمُ الأمير في البلد، وتارةً يكون حَصر موصوف في صفته، نحو: انحصر زيد في البياض، وتارةً يكون حَصرَ وصف في موصوفه نحو انحصر البياض في زيد، وتارةً يكون حَصرَ ظرف في مظروف، نحو: انحصر هذا الإناء في الماء، وتارةً يكون حَصْرَ مظروف في ظرف نحو انحصر الماء في هذا الإناء، إلى غير ذلك، وما نَحْنُ فيه من قبيل الثالث، على أنه لا يستقيم كلام المصنف، ويكون من قبيل الثالث، إلا لو قال المصنف: يَنْحَصِرُ في الوجود والاستحالة والجواز، وهو لم يَقُل ذلك، بل قال: (ينحصر في ثلاثة أقسام أي أقسام للحكم، ولا يخفى أن الثلاثة ليست أقساماً للحكم؛ إذ التقسيم ليس له إلا نوعان: تقسيم الكلّي إلى جزئياته، وتقسيم الكلّ إلى أجزائه، وليس له نوع آخَرُ .
متن أم البراهين لأبي عبد الله السنوسي
٦٣
وكلامُ المصنِّف لا يصحُ من قبيل الأول؛ لعدم صحة الإخبار بالمقسم عن كل قسم من أقسامه؛ إذ لا يصح أن يقال : الوجوبُ حكم عقلي، وكذا البقية؛ لأن الحكم العقلي: إثبات أمرٍ لأمرٍ أو نَفْيُه عنه كما تقدم، ولا شَيْء من ذلك بوجوب ولا استحالةٍ ولا جواز فكيف يَصِحُ الإخبارُ به عن كلّ واحدٍ منها؟ ولا من قبيل الثاني؛ لعدم صِحَّةِ تحليل المُقْسَم إلى أقسامه؛ إذ الوجوب والاستحالةُ والجواز ليست أجزاءً للحكم العقلي، فكيف يَصِحُ تحليله إليها؟ فيتعينُ أن يكون من قبيل الثالث، والمعنى عليه أن الحكم العقليّ لا يَخْرُجُ عن ثلاثة أقسام.
Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi. (His saying: وَالثَّالِثُ حَصْرٌ بِمَعْنَى عَدَمِ الْخُرُوجِ) There is a consideration in making it an alternative to what precedes. The better way is to say: al-hasr (restriction) means عدم الخروج. Then sometimes it is a restriction of a universal (kulli) in its particulars (juz'iyyatih), sometimes a restriction of a whole (kull) in its parts (ajza'ih), sometimes a restriction of a specific relation (muta'allaq khass) – with kasr – in a specific related thing (muta'allaq khass) – with fatha – as in: 'my thought is confined to my sins' and 'the ruler's authority is confined to the city'; sometimes a restriction of a described thing (mawsuf) in its attribute (sifa), as in: 'Zayd is confined to whiteness'; sometimes a restriction of an attribute in its subject (mawsuf), as in: 'whiteness is confined to Zayd'; sometimes a restriction of a container (zarf) in a content (mazruf), as in: 'this vessel is confined to water'; sometimes a restriction of a content (mazruf) in a container (zarf), as in: 'water is confined to this vessel'; and other types. What we are dealing with here is of the third type, even though the author's statement would not be correct, and would be of the third type, only if the author had said: 'it is confined to existence (wujud), impossibility (istihala), and possibility (jawaz).' He did not say that, but said: 'it is confined to three divisions, i.e., divisions of the judgment.' It is not hidden that the three are not divisions of the judgment; for division (taqsim) has only two types: the division of the universal (kulli) into its particulars (juz'iyyatih), and the division of the whole (kull) into its parts (ajza'ih), and there is no other type. The text of Umm al-Barahin by Abu Abd Allah al-Sanusi (page number 63). And the author's statement cannot be valid as the first type because of the invalidity of predicating the divided term (al-muqsam) of each of its divisions; for one cannot say: 'wujub (necessity) is a rational judgment (hukm aqli),' and likewise the rest, because the rational judgment is the affirmation (ithbat) of something for something or its negation (nafy) from it, as has preceded, and none of these [three] is wujub, istihala, or jawaz per se. How then could it be valid to predicate it ('rational judgment') of each one of them? Nor is it valid as the second type because of the invalidity of analyzing the divided term into its divisions; for wujub, istihala, and jawaz are not parts of the rational judgment. How then could it be valid to analyze it into them? Therefore it must be of the third type, and the meaning accordingly is that the rational judgment does not go beyond three categories.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This extensive passage from multiple commentators clarifies why Sanusi's statement that the rational judgment is confined to three categories must be interpreted as the third type of restriction. Al-Anbabi begins by discussing the definition of hasr (restriction) itself, preferring to say it simply means 'non-exiting.' He then enumerates many types of restriction—restricting a universal in its particulars, a whole in its parts, a relation in its correlative, an attribute in its subject, etc.—showing the diversity. He states that Sanusi's case falls under the third type, but then notes a problem: Sanusi did not say 'it is confined to wujud, istihala, and jawaz' directly; he said 'confined to three divisions of the judgment.' Al-Anbabi then points out that division has only two valid forms: dividing a universal into its particulars (like 'animal' into 'human', 'horse') or dividing a whole into its parts (like 'house' into roof, walls). Since wujub, istihala, and jawaz cannot be particulars of the rational judgment (because the rational judgment is defined as affirmation or negation, and these three are qualities), nor can they be parts of the rational judgment, the only remaining possibility is that the statement is of the third type: it is simply saying that rational judgments do not exceed these three categories. He then recapitulates this argument on page 63, explicitly rejecting the first two types and concluding: 'therefore it must be of the third type, and the meaning is that the rational judgment does not go beyond three categories.'

حكم عقلي
وجوب
استحالة
جواز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sanusi's division is a proper logical division of a genus into its species or a whole into its parts.
Correction: It is neither; it is a restriction (hasr) meaning rational judgments do not exceed these three categories.

Checkpoint
Why cannot Sanusi's division be considered a division of a universal into its particulars?
A) Because the three categories (wujub, istihala, jawaz) are not rational judgments themselves.
B) Because the rational judgment can be predicated of them.
C) Because they are parts of the rational judgment.
D) Because there are more than three categories.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi's three categories are not species or parts of the rational judgment but simply indicate its scope.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you categorize the attributes of Allah as wajib, mustahil, or ja'iz, do you think of them as mere intellectual boxes, or do you feel the weight of their implications on your heart? This lesson shows they are a restriction of what reason can know. How does that affect your awe of Allah and your reliance on revelation beyond reason?
?
Al-Anbabi proves that the three categories are not parts of rational judgment but its scope. Is your faith built on a framework that leaves room for mystery, or do you secretly demand that Allah fit into your rational categories? Will you let this lesson humble your intellect before the Divine?
?
If rational judgment is restricted to affirmation or negation, and Sanusi's categories only outline the possibilities, then your mind can never fully grasp Allah's essence. Reflect: Where does your certainty ultimately rest—on your own reasoning or on the transmitted tradition of kalam? What change in your heart must follow from accepting the limits of reason?

Attempted Justification of Sanusi's Division
Text presents the best attempt at justification and al-Ajhuri's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the best attempt to justify Sanusi's division as a first-type division and why it fails due to incomplete coverage of rational judgments.

Core Idea
Adding an implicit word does not fix the problem of covering only affirmations.

Commentators proposed several ways to salvage Sanusi's classification as a proper division of the rational judgment. The best attempt involves assuming an implicit word before each category. But al-Ajhuri points out a fatal flaw: rational judgments include both affirmation and negation, while the three categories cover only affirmations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first the attempt, then the objection.
2
Identify the best attempt
Find the phrase 'أحسنها أنه على تقدير مضاف' and understand what implicit word is assumed. See how it changes the meaning.
The implicit word is 'إثبات' (affirmation) before each category.
3
Understand the objection
Al-Ajhuri objects that rational judgments are not limited to affirmation; they include negation and affirmation of other things. Give an example from the text.
Example of negation: 'لا يجب على الله الصلاح والأصلح'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاول جماعةً تصحيحَ كونه من قبيل الأول بوجوه: منها ما هو بعيد، ومنها ما هو غيرُ سديد، لكن أحْسَنَها أنه على تقدير مضاف قبل قوله: (الوجوب) وما بعده، والأصلُ: إثباتُ الوجوب وإثباتُ الاستحالة وإثباتُ الجواز، وحينئذ صح كونه من قبيل الأول؛ لوجود ضابطه بهذا التقدير؛ إذ يَصِحُ أن يقال: إثبات الوجوب حكم عقليّ وهكذا. فتدبَّرُ.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري
(قوله: وَحَاوَلَ جَمَاعَةٌ ... إلخ) في هذا الجواب نظر؛ لأن الحكم العقلي لا ينحصر في إثبات هذه الثلاثة؛ لأنه قد يكون نفياً، كما في قولنا: لا يجب على الله الصلاح والأصلح، وقد يكون إثباتاً لغيرها، كما في قولنا: الله قادر.
تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي .
فالإشكال على المصنف ليس من حيث الانحصار؛ إذ أقسام الانحصار كثيرة كما قد عَلِمْتَها، إنما الإشكالُ من جَعْل هذه الثلاثة أقساماً للحكم، والأقسام ليس له إلا صفتان: كَوْنُها أقساماً للكلي، فتكون جزئياتٍ له وكونها أقساماً للكل، فهي أجزاء، وبهذا تَعْلَمُ ما في كلام المحبّي أوّلاً وآخراً. تأمل إلا أن يُرَادَ: أقسام منسوبة للحكم من حيث تعليقه بها لا من حيث كونها مقسمها فتدبر.
And a group attempted to correct its being of the first type in several ways: some are far-fetched, some are unsound. But the best of them is that there is an implicit (mudaf) element before his saying: (al-wujub) and what follows, so the original is: 'the affirmation of wujub (ithbat al-wujub), the affirmation of istihala, and the affirmation of jawaz.' In that case, it becomes valid as the first type because its condition is present with this assumption; for it is valid to say: 'the affirmation of wujub is a rational judgment,' and so on. So ponder. The gloss of the scholar Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'And a group attempted... etc.') There is a consideration in this answer, because the rational judgment is not confined to the affirmation of these three; for it may be a negation, as in our saying: 'God is not obliged to do what is beneficial and most beneficial (la yajibu 'ala Allah al-salah wa al-aslah),' and it may be an affirmation of something else, as in our saying: 'God is qadir (able).' Annotations of the scholar Muhammad al-Anbabi: The difficulty regarding the author is not from the aspect of restriction; for the types of restriction are many as you have learned. Rather, the difficulty is from making these three divisions of the judgment. But divisions have only two properties: either they are divisions of a universal, thus being particulars of it, or they are divisions of a whole, thus being parts of it. And by this you know what is in the statement of al-Muhibbi, first and last. Ponder, unless what is intended is 'divisions attributed to the judgment in terms of its relation to them, not in terms of them being its divided thing.' So ponder.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage presents the attempts to salvage Sanusi's division as a proper first-type division. The best attempt is to assume an implicit word before each category: 'affirmation of wujub,' 'affirmation of istihala,' 'affirmation of jawaz.' Then 'affirmation of wujub' is indeed a rational judgment (an affirmation), so the predicate would hold. However, al-Ajhuri objects that rational judgments are not limited to affirmations; they can also be negations, like 'God is not obliged to do the most beneficial.' So the attempt fails because the three categories would only cover affirmations, leaving out negations. Al-Anbabi then reframes the issue: the real problem is not the type of restriction, but that wujub, istihala, and jawaz cannot be divisions (aqsam) of the rational judgment at all, because divisions are either particulars of a universal or parts of a whole, and these three fit neither. He then suggests a different solution: perhaps what is meant is 'divisions relative to the judgment in terms of its relation to them' (i.e., the judgment is connected to these three as its possible values), not that the judgment itself is divided into them. He ends with a reflection. Thus, the attempts to justify the division as the first type are shown to be problematic.

Key Terms
تقدير مضاف
إثبات
نفي
حكم عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adding an implicit word fully solves the problem of division type.
Correction: It only covers affirmations, but rational judgments also include negations and other affirmations.

Checkpoint
What is the best attempt to make Sanusi's division of the first type?
A) To add an implicit word like 'affirmation of' before each category.
B) To reinterpret 'judgment' as 'that which is judged'.
C) To claim that the three categories are parts of the rational judgment.
D) To say that there is only one type of division.

Key Takeaway
The best justification attempt fails because rational judgments include both affirmation and negation, and the three categories only cover affirmations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a simple classification like Sanusi's covers everything, only to later realize you missed important categories? How does this lesson warn you against overconfident categorizations in your own understanding of Islamic theology? What will you do to check for blind spots?
?
When you think about what is rationally necessary, possible, or impossible for God, do you neglect the realm of negation (what God is not) and the full scope of His attributes? How can you ensure your mental framework encompasses all rational judgments, not just affirmations?
?
Al-Ajhuri points out that rational judgments include both affirmation and negation. In your own practice of faith, do you tend to focus on affirming God's attributes while neglecting to negate what is not befitting of Him? How will you balance both in your worship and understanding?

The Deeper Problem: Divisions or Relations?
Al-Anbabi clarifies that the real issue is the nature of division and proposes a relational interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the three categories cannot be divisions of the rational judgment at all, and how a relational interpretation might resolve the issue.

Core Idea
Wujub, istihala, and jawaz are not divisions but values in relation to the judgment.

Al-Anbabi deepens the analysis: the real problem is not about restriction or coverage, but about the nature of division itself. He explains that divisions are either particulars of a universal or parts of a whole. The three categories fit neither, so a different interpretation is needed.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Anbabi's text
Read the passage from 'تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي' to the end. Note his restatement of the issue.
2
Understand the two types of division
Al-Anbabi says divisions have only two properties: they are either divisions of a universal (as particulars) or divisions of a whole (as parts). Find these in the text.
The text: 'الأقسام ليس له إلا صفتان: كونها أقساماً للكلي، فتكون جزئياتٍ له وكونها أقساماً للكل، فهي أجزاء'
3
Apply to the three categories
Ask: Are wujub, istihala, and jawaz particulars of a universal 'rational judgment'? Or are they parts of the whole? Why does al-Anbabi say they don't fit? Then consider the alternative: perhaps they are divisions in a relational sense.
The alternative interpretation: 'أقسام منسوبة للحكم من حيث تعليقه بها لا من حيث كونها مقسمها'

Source Text

Checkpoint

A) They do not cover all rational judgments.
B) They are neither particulars of a universal nor parts of a whole.
C) They are not affirmed of God.
D) They are not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The three categories are not proper divisions of the judgment; they are better understood as values related to the judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might this lesson correct a careless assumption you have made about such categories? Will you re-examine one this week?
?
Al-Anbabi says the three modalities are 'divisions attributed to the judgment in terms of its relation to them.' What will you do to correct that in your dua or reflection?
?
The author ends with 'ponder.' What is one area of your religious knowledge where you have accepted a classification without questioning its logical validity? How will you investigate its structure using the criteria from this lesson? Commit to one action today.

The Three Rational Judgments: Wujub, Istihalah, and Jawaz
Al-Bajuri defines the three rational judgments and explains they are mental considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the three rational judgments (wujub, istihalah, jawaz) and why they are considered mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya) rather than real attributes.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are mental tools, not real attributes of things.

Before we can reason about God and creation, we need a clear vocabulary for describing what is necessary, impossible, or possible. Al-Bajuri begins by defining these three categories precisely, showing that they are not properties inherent in things but rational judgments we apply to concepts. This foundational lesson establishes the grammar of all theological argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how each definition is structured around 'acceptance' (qabul) or 'non-acceptance'.
2
Understand the alternation in jawaz
Jawaz is defined as acceptance of both existence and non-existence, but alternately, not simultaneously. Think about why simultaneous acceptance would be impossible.
This nuance is important to avoid misunderstanding possibility as mere ambiguity.
3
Grasp the 'prevention' interpretation
The passage offers an alternative interpretation using 'imtina'' (prevention). This aligns with the view that these are mental considerations, not mere negations.
This refutes the idea that wujub and istihalah are purely negative.
4
Connect to the bigger picture
Ask yourself: Why does it matter that these are 'i'tibarat 'aqliyya'? How does this affect our understanding of God's attributes?
It prevents us from imagining these categories as real entities alongside God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الْوُجُوبُ) وهو عدم قبول الانتفاء. والْاِسْتِحَالَةُ) هي عدم قبول الثبوت. والْجَوَازُ) هو قَبُولُهما لكن على سبيل التناوب، بمعنى: قبول الثبوت تارةً وقبول الانتفاء تارةً أخرى لا على سبيل الاجتماع؛ إذ لا يُمْكِنُ قَبولهما معاً. لك أن تُفَسِّرَهُ: بامتناع قبول الانتفاء، والاستحالة بامتناع قبول الثبوت، بل هو الموافق لما قاله الغُنَيْمِيُّ من أن الوجوب والاستحالة والجواز اعتبارات عقلية، وعليه فيندفع ما قرَّرَهُ بعضهم من أن الوجوب والاستحالة أمران سلبيان، والجواز أمر اعتباري أُخِذَ بظاهر عَدَم كذا وعدم كذا في الأولين، وقبولِ كذا في الأخير.
Al-wujub (necessity) is non-acceptance of non-existence. Al-istihalah (impossibility) is non-acceptance of existence. Al-jawaz (possibility) is acceptance of both, but on the basis of alternation, meaning: acceptance of existence sometimes and acceptance of non-existence at other times, not on the basis of conjunction, since it is impossible to accept both together. You may interpret it: as the prevention of acceptance of non-existence, and istihalah as the prevention of acceptance of existence; indeed this is in agreement with what al-Ghunaimi said: that wujub, istihalah, and jawaz are mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya). On this basis, what some have asserted is refuted: that wujub and istihalah are two negative matters, and jawaz is a relational matter taken from the apparent meaning of 'not this and not that' in the first two, and 'acceptance of that' in the last.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage defines the three rational judgments—wujub, istihalah, and jawaz—which are the foundational categories in kalam for describing modal status. The author begins by giving the standard definitions: wujub is the inability to be cancelled (i.e., necessary existence cannot cease), istihalah is the inability to be affirmed (impossible existence cannot occur), and jawaz is the ability to receive both existence and non-existence alternately, not simultaneously. A subtle point follows: one may interpret these definitions as 'prevention of acceptance' rather than simple negation, which aligns with al-Ghunaimi's view that these three are mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya)—they are not real attributes inhering in things but rational judgments applied by the mind. This refutes the claim that wujub and istihalah are purely negative concepts; rather, they are positive mental determinations. The discussion ensures that the student understands the ontological status of these categories: they are not independent entities but tools of analysis.

Misconception: Wujub and istihalah are purely negative concepts (salb) with no positive content.
Correction: The passage argues they are positive mental considerations, as shown by the alternative interpretation using 'prevention' (imtina'), which implies a positive judgment by the mind.

The Definition of Jawaz and the Order of the Three Judgments
Al-Bajuri defines jawaz and explains the order of wujub, istihalah, jawaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of jawaz as alternating possibility and the rational reasons for the order of wujub, istihalah, and jawaz.

Core Idea
The three judgments follow a logical order: nobility, opposition, and simplicity.

In this passage, Al-Bajuri clarifies two important points: the exact definition of jawaz as possibility that alternates between existence and non-existence, and the reasoning behind the sequence in which the three judgments are presented. This structure is not arbitrary but based on conceptual priority and pedagogical wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Al-Bajuri first defines jawaz, then explains the order.
2
Understand the definition of jawaz
Focus on the definition: jawaz is acceptance of both existence and non-existence on an alternating basis, not simultaneously. Consider why it cannot be both at once.
The term 'tanaawub' (alternation) is key.
3
Analyze the order of the three judgments
Identify the three reasons given: nobility of wujub, opposition of istihalah, and the composite nature of jawaz. Ask yourself why each reason justifies its position.
The order is pedagogical: from simple to composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: وَالْجَوَازُ) هو قَبُولُهما لكن على سبيل التناوب، بمعنى: قبول الثبوت تارةً وقبول الانتفاء تارةً أخرى لا على سبيل الاجتماع؛ إذ لا يُمْكِنُ قَبولهما معاً. وقَدَّمَ الوجوبَ لشَرَفه، وأعْقَبَه بالاستحالة؛ لأنها ضده، والضد أقرب الأشياء خُطُوراً بالبال عند ذِكْرِ ضِدّه، وأَخَّرَ الجواز؛ لأنه لم يَبْقَ له مرتبة إلا التأخير، وأيضاً فهو شبيه بالمركَّب، وما قبله شبية بالبسيط، والمركب متأخّر عن البسيط.
And his saying: 'And al-jawaz is acceptance of both, but on the basis of alternation, meaning: acceptance of existence sometimes and acceptance of non-existence at other times, not on the basis of conjunction, since it is impossible to accept both together.' And he placed al-wujub first due to its nobility, and followed it with al-istihalah because it is its opposite, and the opposite is the most prominent thing that comes to mind when its opposite is mentioned. And he delayed al-jawaz because no rank remains for it except delay, and also it resembles the composite, while what precedes it resembles the simple, and the composite is subsequent to the simple.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage clarifies the definition of jawaz by emphasizing its alternating nature: jawaz means a thing can either exist or not exist, but not both at once. The author then explains the rationale for the order in which the three modal judgments are presented—wujub first, then istihalah, then jawaz. The reasons are: (1) Wujub comes first due to its nobility (sharaf), since necessary existence is the highest attribute. (2) Istihalah follows immediately because it is the direct opposite (didd) of wujub, and the mind naturally juxtaposes opposites. (3) Jawaz is placed last because it has no higher rank, and also because it is analogous to a composite (murakkab) while the first two are simple (basit); the composite naturally comes after the simple. This ordering is both logical and didactic.

Key Terms

الاستحالة
الجواز
التناوب
الضد
المركب
البسيط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The order of the three judgments is arbitrary or based on alphabetical order.
Correction: Al-Bajuri explains specific reasons: wujub due to nobility, istihalah due to opposition, jawaz due to being composite and having no higher rank.

Checkpoint

B) Because istihalah is the opposite (didd) of wujub, and opposites are recalled together
C) Because istihalah is more noble than wujub
D) Because the text requires alphabetical order

Key Takeaway
Jawaz is the alternating possibility of existence and non-existence, and the traditional order of the three judgments is reasoned: wujub (nobility), istihalah (opposition), jawaz (composite and residual).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says necessity is 'noble' and impossibility is its opposite. Reflect on a time you treated a contingent matter as if it were necessary, demanding it must happen. How did that reveal a lack of tawwakul? What step can you take tomorrow to surrender that demand?
?
Jawaz is described as composite, containing both existence and non-existence. Is there a situation in your heart where you are trying to hold onto both possibilities (e.g., safety and risk, wealth and poverty) instead of accepting one of them from Allah? Why Define Derived Terms? The Objection and Its Answer
Al-Bajuri answers the objection on defining derived terms with predication logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reason Al-Sanusi defined the derived terms (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) instead of their sources, and how this reflects the logical structure of kalam predication.

Core Idea
In kalam, the derived term is the predicate, so defining it is more direct.

Sanusi defines the three rational judgments using their derived names (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) rather than the source nouns (wujub, istihalah, jawaz). This might seem inconsistent because he introduced the sources first. Bajuri anticipates this objection and provides a defense rooted in the nature of predication. We will examine the objection, the answer, and the deeper reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the objection and answer. Note the flow: objection, then answer, then deeper wisdom.
The fa in 'faliwajibu' is for clarification, not branching.
2
Identify the objection
Why would one say Sanusi should have defined the sources? Because he mentioned wujub etc. first, but then defined wajib etc.
The objection highlights an apparent inconsistency.
3
Understand the answer
The answer says defining the derived terms suffices because they are derived from the sources and because later usage in the text employs the derived forms.
The marginal commentary elaborates on this.
4
Explore the hikmah
The deeper reason: in propositions of identical predication (haml muwata'ah), the predicate is the derived term. So defining the derived term aligns with how it is actually used.
Example: 'Allah's knowledge is wajib' – 'wajib' is the predicate.
5
Apply the insight
Consider why this distinction matters for understanding kalam texts. It shows that definitions are chosen to match the logical role of terms in arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: فَالْوَاجِبُ... إلخ) أي: إذا أردتَ بيان كلّ من هذه الأمور الثلاثة فالواجبُ إلخ، فالفاء للإفصاح لا للتفريع. فإن قيل: كان المناسِبُ للمصنف: أن يُعَرِّفَ كلّاً من الوجوب والاستحالة والجواز، لا كلّاً من الواجب والمستحيل والجائز؛ لأنه ذَكَرَ أولاً الوجوبَ وأَخَوَيْه دون الواجب وأخويه، فقد ذَكَرَ شيئاً ولم يُعَرِّفه، وعرف شيئاً ولم يذكرْهُ. أجيب بأنه استغنى بتعريف الواجب وأخويه عن تعريف الوجوب وأخويه؛ لأن الواجب مشتق من الوجوب وهكذا ... حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري . (قوله: أجيب... إلخ) هذا الجواب مصحح للعدول عن تعريف المصادر الثلاثة إلى تعريف المشتقات الثلاثة، والمرجح لهذا العدول أن المذكور فيما يأتي هو المشتقات كقوله الآتي: (فمما يجب لمولانا جل وعز عشرون صفة). وقوله: (و مما يستحيل في حقه تعالى عشرون صفة. وقوله: (وأما الجائز في حقه تعالى ففعل كل ممكن أو تركه. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي. (قوله: أُجِيبَ بِأَنَّهُ اسْتَغْنَى... إلخ) وحكمة عدوله عن تعريف الوجوب وأَخَوَيه إلى تعريف الواجب وأخويه هي أن المحمول في القضية حَمْلَ مواطأة هو الواجب وأخواه، فيقال: عِلْمُ الله واجب، وشريكه مستحيل، ورِزْقُهُ جائز.
His saying: 'So the wajib... etc.' i.e., when you intend to explain each of these three matters, [you say] 'the wajib is...' etc., so the fa is for clarification, not for branching. If it is said: It would have been more appropriate for the author to define each of wujub, istihalah, and jawaz, not each of wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz; because he first mentioned wujub and its two siblings, not wajib and its two siblings. Thus he mentioned something and did not define it, and defined something and did not mention it. It is answered: He dispensed with defining wujub and its siblings by defining wajib and its siblings, because wajib is derived from wujub, and so on... (Marginal commentary of al-'Allamah Ahmad al-Ujhuri). (His saying: 'It is answered... etc.') This answer corrects the deviation from defining the three sources to defining the three derived terms, and the preponderating reason for this deviation is that what is mentioned later are the derived terms, such as his later statement: 'Among that which is wajib for our Lord, Exalted and Majestic, are twenty attributes,' and his statement: 'Among that which is mustahil with respect to Him, Exalted, are twenty attributes,' and his statement: 'As for what is ja'iz with respect to Him, Exalted, it is either doing every possible thing or refraining from it.' (Notes of al-'Allamah Muhammad al-Anbabi). (His saying: 'It is answered that he dispensed with... etc.') And the wisdom of his deviation from defining wujub and its siblings to defining wajib and its siblings is that the predicate in the proposition, in terms of 'haml muwata'ah' (identical predication), is wajib and its siblings. Thus it is said: 'The knowledge of Allah is wajib, His partner is mustahil, and His provision is ja'iz.'
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection regarding Sanusi's choice to define the derived terms (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) instead of the source terms (wujub, istihalah, jawaz) that were previously mentioned. The objection is that Sanusi mentioned wujub etc. but then defined wajib etc., which seems inconsistent. The answer, as provided by the marginal commentators, is twofold. First, the derived terms are sufficient because they are directly derived from the sources—defining the derived term implicitly defines the source. Second, and more importantly, the later discussion in the text (e.g., enumerating attributes as wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) uses the derived terms, so defining them here is more directly useful for the subsequent material. Additionally, in propositions like 'Allah's knowledge is wajib,' the predicate is 'wajib' (the derived form), not 'wujub,' because predication is identity (haml muwata'ah)—the predicate is exactly the attribute assigned to the subject. Thus, defining the derived terms aligns with their actual use in kalam discourse.

المستحيل
الجائز
الوجوب
الاستحالة
الجواز
المشتقات
المصادر
حمل المواطأة

Correction: Sanusi's choice is deliberate: defining the derived terms is sufficient and pedagogically sound because the derived terms are the predicates in actual propositions, and their definitions are directly applicable to later discussions.

Definition of al-Jawaz and Ordering of the Three Judgments
Bajuri defines jawaz and gives reasons for the order of the three judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-jawaz as alternation between existence and non-existence, and the rationale behind the order of the three rational judgments.

Core Idea
Al-jawaz is alternating possibility; its last place reflects its composite nature.

Bajuri now turns to the third rational judgment, al-jawaz (permissibility). He defines it clearly, emphasizing that it involves accepting existence and non-existence alternately, not simultaneously. Then he explains why the three judgments are ordered as they are: wujub first, istihala second, jawaz last. This ordering is not arbitrary but based on logical and rhetorical principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of jawaz
Read the first part of the passage where Bajuri defines al-jawaz as 'acceptance of both existence and non-existence on an alternate basis'. Note that he stresses alternation, not simultaneity.
2
Grasp the alternation concept
Reflect on why accepting both together is impossible. This is a key point to avoid misunderstanding.
The common mistake is to think jawaz means simultaneous possibility.
3
Learn the first reason for ordering
Bajuri explains that wujub is presented first due to its honor, and istihala follows because it is the opposite of wujub, and opposites are naturally recalled together.
4
Learn the second reason for ordering
He adds that jawaz is delayed because it resembles the composite, whereas wujub and istihala are simple. The composite naturally comes after the simple.
This is both a logical and a rhetorical point.
5
Reflect on the overall logic
Consider the combined reasons: honor, opposition, and composition. How do they together make the order unambiguously correct?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: وَالْجَوَازُ) هو قَبُولُهما لكن على سبيل التناوب، بمعنى: قبول الثبوت تارةً وقبول الانتفاء تارةً أخرى لا على سبيل الاجتماع؛ إذ لا يُمْكِنُ قَبولهما معاً. وقَدَّمَ الوجوبَ لشَرَفه، وأعْقَبَه بالاستحالة؛ لأنها ضده، والضد أقرب الأشياء خُطُوراً بالبال عند ذِكْرِ ضِدّه، وأَخَّرَ الجواز؛ لأنه لم يَبْقَ له مرتبة إلا التأخير، وأيضاً فهو شبيه بالمركَّب، وما قبله شبية بالبسيط، والمركب متأخّر عن البسيط.
And his saying: 'And al-jawaz (permissibility)' means acceptance of both (existence and non-existence) but on an alternate basis, i.e., acceptance of existence at one time and acceptance of non-existence at another, not simultaneously, because accepting both together is impossible. And he presented necessity (al-wujub) first due to its honor, and followed it with impossibility (al-istihala) because it is its opposite, and the opposite is the closest thing to the mind when its opposite is mentioned, and he delayed permissibility (al-jawaz) because no rank remained for it except delay, and also because it resembles the composite, while what precedes it resembles the simple, and the composite is subsequent to the simple.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the third rational judgment, al-jawaz. The author first defines jawaz as the acceptance of both existence and non-existence, but on an alternating basis, not simultaneously. He then explains the order of the three judgments: wujub (necessity) is presented first due to its honor, istihala (impossibility) is second because it is the opposite of necessity, and the opposite is naturally recalled when mentioning something. Jawaz is delayed because it is like the composite (including both possibility of existence and non-existence) while the others are simple, and the composite is naturally subsequent. This illustrates the logical and rhetorical reasoning behind the arrangement.

Misconception: Al-jawaz means accepting both existence and non-existence simultaneously.
Correction: Bajuri clarifies that it is acceptance on an alternate basis, not simultaneously, because simultaneous acceptance is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Al-jawaz is the alternating acceptance of existence and non-existence, and its placement last is justified by its composite nature and the logical priority of opposites.

Distinguishing Wujub in Tawhid from Common Usage
Bajuri distinguishes theological wujub from juridical wujub.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinct meaning of 'wujub' in the science of tawhid versus its juridical sense, and why confusing them leads to error.

Core Idea
God's wajib is logical; man's wajib is legal.

The term 'wajib' appears in both kalam and fiqh, but its meaning shifts completely. In tawhid, it refers to what cannot be non-existent—like God's existence. In fiqh, it refers to what earns reward or punishment. This lesson clarifies that distinction through Bajuri's concise warning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then its translation. Notice how Bajuri contrasts two contexts for the same term.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the two meanings
Locate the definition of wujub in tawhid (implied from earlier context) and the 'well-known meaning' (common usage).
The well-known meaning is the fiqhi one.
3
Understand the exception
Bajuri notes that when saying 'it is obligatory upon the responsible person to know', wujub carries the common meaning, not the theological one.
This exception prevents misreading statements about human obligations.
4
Reflect on the warning
Bajuri ends by warning against confusion. Ask yourself: what error does this distinction prevent?
The error is saying something insubstantial, like claiming God has duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الوجوب بذلك المعنى هو المراد في علم التوحيد متى أُطلِق، إلا في نحو قولهم: يجب على كلّ مكلَّف أن يَعْرِفَ... إلخ، فهو فيه بالمعنى المشهور، وهو كَوْنُ الشيء بحيث يُثَابُ على فعله ويعاقب على تركه، ففَرْقٌ بين أن يقال: الله كذا وأن يقال: يجب على المكلف كذا فاخرض على هذا الفرق، ولا تكُنْ من اشتبه عليه الأمرُ فقال ما لا مُحَصَّلَ له.
And know that wujub (necessity) in that meaning [i.e., the meaning explained earlier regarding the rational judgment] is what is intended in the science of tawhid whenever it is used absolutely, except in the statement 'it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person to know...' etc., for in that context it is used in the well-known meaning, which is that a thing is such that one is rewarded for doing it and punished for omitting it. So there is a difference between saying 'God is such' and saying 'it is obligatory upon the responsible person to be such'. So pay attention to this difference, and do not be among those for whom the matter is confused and who say something insubstantial.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a crucial clarification regarding the term 'wujub'. In 'ilm al-tawhid, when we say something is 'wajib' of God, it means logical necessity (that non-existence is inconceivable). But in fiqh, 'wajib' refers to a moral obligation rewarded or punished. The author warns against confusing these two meanings, especially when one says 'it is obligatory upon the responsible person to know' — here it is the fiqhi meaning, not the theological one. This prevents a common misunderstanding where someone might think that God is under obligation, which is not the case.

Key Terms
الوجوب
علم التوحيد
المعنى المشهور
الثواب والعقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'wajib' in tawhid (e.g., God is wajib al-wujud) means the same as 'wajib' in fiqh (e.g., prayer is wajib).
Correction: In tawhid, wujub refers to the impossibility of non-existence; in fiqh, it refers to an act whose doer is rewarded and whose omittor is punished. They are different categories.

Checkpoint

B) Logical necessity, i.e., impossibility of non-existence
C) Moral obligation
D) Customary practice

Key Takeaway
The term 'wajib' has two distinct meanings: logical necessity in tawhid and legal obligation in fiqh; mixing them leads to error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage warns against being among those who say 'something insubstantial' due to confusion. Reflect: In your own learning, where have you been careless with theological terms, speaking without precision? How can you commit to clarity in your understanding of Allah's attributes?
?
Consider how you view your daily obligations (prayer, fasting) and compare that to your understanding of God's necessity. Are you treating God's attributes as if they were legal commands? How does this distinction change the way you worship—seeing Him as Necessary Being rather than as One who merely commands?

Why Define Derived Terms? The Objection and Answer
Bajuri addresses why the definitions are given for al-wajib, al-mustahil, al-ja'iz instead of al-wujub, al-istihala, al-jawaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam scholars define derived terms like al-wajib and why this reveals an important distinction between types of predication.

Core Idea
Defining derived terms implies the sources and fits how we actually make statements about Allah.

This lesson addresses a subtle but important objection: why did the author define the derived terms (al-wajib, al-mustahil, al-ja'iz) instead of the source terms (al-wujub, al-istihala, al-jawaz) he first mentioned? The answer reveals not only a logical economy but also a key insight into how predication works in Arabic. As you work through the passage, you'll see how this choice is perfectly suited for theological discourse.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the objector's point: inconsistency between mentioning sources but defining derivatives.
2
Understand the answer's first part
The answer says defining the derivative suffices because it implies the source. Why? Because it's derived from it.
3
Grasp the second part about predication
Identify the two types of predication: essential predication (حمل المواطأة) and predicative attribution (حمل الاشتقاق). See how the derived terms are the actual predicates in propositions.
4
Connect to the author's final note
The author mentions that the source terms are the intended focus, even though the derived terms are used. Why does he say that?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: كان المناسِبُ للمصنف: أن يُعَرِّفَ كلّاً من الوجوب والاستحالة والجواز، لا كلّاً من الواجب والمستحيل والجائز؛ لأنه ذَكَرَ أولاً الوجوبَ وأَخَوَيْه دون الواجب وأخويه، فقد ذَكَرَ شيئاً ولم يُعَرِّفه، وعرف شيئاً ولم يذكرْهُ. أجيب بأنه استغنى بتعريف الواجب وأخويه عن تعريف الوجوب وأخويه؛ لأن الواجب مشتق من الوجوب وهكذا ... وحكمة عدوله عن تعريف الوجوب وأَخَوَيه إلى تعريف الواجب وأخويه هي أن المحمول في القضية حَمْلَ مواطأة هو الواجب وأخواه، فيقال: عِلْمُ الله واجب، وشريكه مستحيل، ورِزْقُهُ جائز. وحَمْلُ المواطأة: هو ما لا يحتاج لتأويل كما ذكره، ويقابِلُهُ: حَمْلُ الاشتقاق: وهو ما لا يحتاجُ لذلك، كقولك: الإمام الشافعي عِلْمٌ ، أي : ذُو عِلْمٍ أو عالم، والقُطنُ بياضُ أي: ذو بياض أو أبيضُ، وإنما لم يَقُل من أول الأمر وينحصر في ثلاثة الواجب إلخ؛ لأن الوجوب وأخويه هو المقصود والملتفت إليه.
For it is said: What was appropriate for the author was to define each of al-wujub, al-istihala, and al-jawaz, not each of al-wajib, al-mustahil, and al-ja'iz, because he first mentioned al-wujub and its two siblings without al-wajib and its siblings; so he mentioned something without defining it, and defined something without mentioning it. The answer is that he sufficed by defining al-wajib and its siblings from defining al-wujub and its siblings; because al-wajib is derived from al-wujub, and so on. And the wisdom in his turning from defining al-wujub and its siblings to defining al-wajib and its siblings is that the predicate in a proposition of essential predication is al-wajib and its siblings; so it is said: 'Knowledge of God is wajib (necessary), His partner is mustahil (impossible), and His provision is ja'iz (possible).' And essential predication is that which does not require interpretation, as mentioned; its counterpart is predicative attribution, which does require interpretation, such as saying: 'Imam al-Shafi'i is knowledge,' meaning 'possessor of knowledge' or 'knowledgeable,' and 'cotton is whiteness,' meaning 'possessor of whiteness' or 'white.' And he did not say from the beginning 'and it is confined to three: al-wajib, etc.' because al-wujub and its siblings are the intended and focused upon.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector notes that the author first mentioned the source terms (al-wujub, al-istihala, al-jawaz) but then defined the derived terms (al-wajib, al-mustahil, al-ja'iz). This seems inconsistent. The answer provides two reasons: first, the derived term implies knowledge of the source because it is derived from it. Second, the actual predicate in propositions is the derived term (e.g., 'knowledge is wajib'), not the source. The author then explains the two types of predication: essential predication (حمل مواطأة) where the predicate is identical in meaning without need for interpretation, and predicative attribution (حمل اشتقاق) which requires interpreting the predicate as having a relation (e.g., 'Zayd is justice' meaning 'just'). The author concludes that the source terms are what is ultimately intended, but the derived terms are used because they function as predicates.

الاستحالة
المستحيل
الجواز
الجائز
حمل المواطأة
حمل الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Defining derived terms instead of source terms is a mistake or insufficient.
Correction: It is not a mistake; it is more precise because derived terms are the actual predicates used in theological statements, and they logically imply the source terms.

Checkpoint

B) Because the predicate in propositions is al-wajib and knowing it implies knowing al-wujub
C) Because al-wujub is not used in kalam
D) Because he forgot

Key Takeaway
The author's choice to define derived terms is both sufficient and precise because they serve as predicates in statements about Allah and imply their source terms.

?
The passage shows that the author deliberately used derived terms because they are the actual predicates in our statements about Allah. Are your statements about Allah's attributes merely academic repetitions, or do they spring from a heart that truly knows these attributes are real? Commit to one attribute today and speak it as a direct reality, then act on it.

Deriving the Definitions of Wajib, Mustahil, and Ja'iz
Bajuri defines the three rational judgments by deriving them from their source terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definitions of the three rational judgments are derived from their derived terms and what each judgment means in the mind.

Core Idea
The definitions of wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz are derived from the derived terms wujub, istihala, and jawaz.

In this passage, Bajuri explains the link between the derived terms (al-wajib, al-mustahil, al-ja'iz) and their source concepts (al-wujub, al-istihala, al-jawaz). By understanding how the derived term implies the source, we can arrive at precise definitions of each rational judgment. Let's see how he defines wujub, istihala, and jawaz as mental states.

Guided Path
1
Understand the derived-term principle
Bajuri states that knowing the derived term (e.g., al-wajib) necessarily implies knowing the source term (al-wujub) because the derived term contains the source.
This is a key linguistic principle in kalam: the derived term is defined by its source.
2
Definition of wujub
From the definition of al-wajib, we derive that wujub is the inconceivability of non-existence in the mind.
‘Inconceivability’ means the mind cannot conceive its non-existence.
3
Definition of istihala
Similarly, from al-mustahil, istihala is the inconceivability of existence in the mind.
4
Definition of jawaz
From al-ja'iz, jawaz is the validity of both existence and non-existence in the mind.
Validity here means the mind accepts both as possible.
5
Clarification of the preposition 'fi'
Bajuri notes that ‘fi’ in ‘fi al-aql’ can mean ‘at’ (if tasawwur is possibility) or ‘by’ (if tasawwur is perception).
This shows the precision of kalam terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و معرفةُ المشتقِّ تستلزمُ معرفة المشتق منه ؛ لأنه جُزْؤُهُ؛ إذ الواجب أمرٌ موصوف بالوجوب وهكذا. (قوله: مَا لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ) بِضَمَّ الْيَاءِ مبنى لما لم يُسَمَّ فاعله، بمعنى: لا يُدْرَكُ أو بفتحها مبنياً للفاعل، بمعنى: لا يُمْكِنُ (قوله: وَمَعْرِفَةُ الْمُشْتَقِ... إلخ) فيعرف من تعريف الواجب بما ذكره: أن وجوب الشيء عدم تصور عدمه في العقل ومن تعريف المستحيل بما ذكره أن استحالة الشيء عدم تصور وجوده في العقل، ومن تعريف الجائز بما ذكره أن جواز الشيء: صحة وجوده وعدمه في العقل، و(في) بمعنى (عند) إن جُعِلَ التصور بمعنى الإمكان، وبمعنى باء السببية إن جعل التصور بمعنى الإدراك.
And knowledge of the derived term necessarily entails knowledge of the source term, because it is part of it; for the wajib is a thing described with wujub, and so on. (His saying: 'ما لا يتصور' with damm on the ya' as passive participle, meaning 'is not perceived', or with fat-h as active participle, meaning 'is not possible'). (His saying: 'And knowledge of the derived term... etc.') So it is known from the definition of al-wajib as he mentioned that the wujub of a thing is the inconceivability of its non-existence in the mind; and from the definition of al-mustahil as he mentioned that the istihala of a thing is the inconceivability of its existence in the mind; and from the definition of al-ja'iz as he mentioned that the jawaz of a thing is the validity of its existence and non-existence in the mind. And the word 'fi' (في) means 'at' if tasawwur is taken as possibility, and it means the causative 'ba' if tasawwur is taken as perception.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage explains the definitions of the three rational judgments by showing what each implies. The author first notes that knowing the derived term (al-wajib) implies knowing the source (al-wujub) because the derived term includes the source. Then he gives the definitions: wujub is that the non-existence of the thing is not conceivable in the mind; istihala is that its existence is not conceivable; jawaz is that both existence and non-existence are valid in the mind. He also clarifies the preposition 'fi' in the expression 'in the mind'—it can mean 'at' or 'by' depending on the interpretation of tasawwur as possibility or perception. This shows the technical precision of kalam definitions.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think jawaz means that both existence and non-existence are equally possible without restriction in external reality.
Correction: According to the text, jawaz is about validity in the mind, not external reality. The definition specifies 'في العقل' (in the mind), indicating that it is a rational judgment about conceivability.

Checkpoint

C) The existence of the thing is not conceivable

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments are defined by the conceivability or lack thereof of existence and non-existence in the mind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Objection: Conceiving Non-Existence of the Necessary Being
Bajuri addresses an objection on the definition of wajib and clarifies the role of mental conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that the mind can conceive the non-existence of a necessary being is resolved by distinguishing between mere imagination and rational belief.

Core Idea
Imagining the impossible does not make it rationally possible.

In defining the necessary being (wajib) as that whose non-existence is inconceivable, an objection is raised: can't we imagine God's power absent? Bajuri's response sharpens a critical distinction in kalam between mere mental representation (tasawwur) and actual belief (tasdiq). This lesson unpacks that objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the challenge to the definition of wajib: the mind can conceive the non-existence of a wajib because it can conceive the impossible.
Notice the example given: imagining if God's power were absent.
2
Examine the response
Note the distinction between conception (tasawwur) and belief (tasdiq). The response clarifies that such mental images are without belief.
This is a key solution to many similar objections.
3
Apply the distinction
Understand why the definition remains sound: it refers to rational impossibility, not mere imaginative possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتُرِضَ بأن الواجب قد يتصوَّر في العقل عَدَمُه؛ إذ العقل قد يتصوَّرُ المحال. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: قَدْ يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْل عَدَمُه كما لو قيل: لو انتفت قدرة الله تعالى؛ لم يوجد شيء من العالم. فإن قال قائل : متصور عدم القدرة أي مخطر له بباله من غير تصديق به. تقريرات العلامة محمد الأنبابي.
And it was objected that the wajib (necessary) might have its non-existence conceived in the mind, because the mind may conceive the impossible. Annotation of al-'Allamah Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'its non-existence may be conceived in the mind') as if it is said: if the power of God were absent, nothing of the world would exist. So if someone says: 'The non-existence of power is conceived,' meaning it occurs to the mind without belief. Annotations of al-'Allamah Muhammad al-Anbabi.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objection is that the definition of wajib as 'that whose non-existence is inconceivable' seems false because the mind can conceive of the non-existence of God's power, for example, by imagining 'if God's power were absent, the world would not exist.' The response clarifies that such conception is merely a mental representation without belief (tasawwur without tasdiq). The mind can imagine impossibilities, but this does not mean they are considered possible or that the definition is invalid. The definition refers to the impossibility of conceiving non-existence in the sense of rational acceptance, not mere fantasy. This distinction is crucial for understanding kalam definitions.

Key Terms
الواجب
التصوّر
المحال
التصور العقلي
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that imagining the non-existence of God's power challenges the definition of wajib as that whose non-existence cannot be conceived.
Correction: The passage shows that such imagining is mere mental representation without belief; it does not undermine the definition, which concerns rational impossibility.

Checkpoint

B) By stating that such conception is mere imagination without belief
C) By changing the definition
D) By saying it is a different kind of wajib

Key Takeaway
The definition of wajib concerns rational belief, not mere imagination; conceiving non-existence in the mind without belief does not violate the definition.

The Three Rational Judgments from Definitions
Al-Bajuri derives the three rational judgments from earlier definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definitions of necessary, impossible, and possible produce the three rational judgments and the role of mental conception in determining them.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are defined by what the mind can or cannot conceive.

Al-Bajuri now draws out the consequences of the earlier definitions. He shows that each of the three categories—necessary, impossible, and possible—has a clear logical meaning when understood in relation to mental conception. This passage is the key to understanding how kalam reasoning classifies all propositions about existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the parallel structure: each judgment is defined by what the mind conceives.
The passage is short but dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three derivations
See how each definition (واجب, مستحيل, جائز) yields a specific rational judgment (وجوب, استحالة, جواز). Note the key phrases: 'عدم تصور عدمه' for wujūb, 'عدم تصور وجوده' for istiḥāla, and 'صحة وجوده وعدمه' for jawāz.
3
Consider the clarification about 'fī'
Al-Bajuri distinguishes two interpretations of the preposition 'fī' depending on the meaning of 'taṣawwur'. Think about why this precision matters for the overall argument.
This guards against misunderstandings about the relationship between the judgment and the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيعرف من تعريف الواجب بما ذكره: أن وجوب الشيء عدم تصور عدمه في العقل ومن تعريف المستحيل بما ذكره أن استحالة الشيء عدم تصور وجوده في العقل، ومن تعريف الجائز بما ذكره أن جواز الشيء: صحة وجوده وعدمه في العقل، و(في) بمعنى (عند) إن جُعِلَ التصور بمعنى الإمكان، وبمعنى باء السببية إن جعل التصور بمعنى الإدراك.
So it is known from the definition of the necessary (wajib) as he mentioned: that the necessity (wujūb) of a thing is the non-conceivability of its non-existence in the mind, and from the definition of the impossible (mustaḥīl) as he mentioned: that the impossibility (istiḥāla) of a thing is the non-conceivability of its existence in the mind, and from the definition of the possible (jā’iz) as he mentioned: that the possibility (jawāz) of a thing is the validity of its existence and non-existence in the mind, and (fī) is in the meaning of (ʿinda) if conception (taṣawwur) is taken in the sense of possibility (imkān), and in the meaning of the bā’ of causation if conception is taken in the sense of perception (idrāk).
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage explains how the definitions of the three rational judgments are derived from the earlier definitions of wājib, mustaḥīl, and jā’iz. It is a concise exposition of the core logical structure used in kalām for classifying all objects of thought. The author first states that from the definition of wājib (the necessary existent) we learn that its necessity consists in the mind’s inability to conceive its non-existence. Then from the definition of mustaḥīl (the impossible) we learn that its impossibility consists in the mind’s inability to conceive its existence. Then from the definition of jā’iz (the possible) we learn that its possibility consists in the mind’s valid conception of both its existence and non-existence. The author then clarifies the preposition 'fī' in the original definitions: if taṣawwur (conception) is taken in the sense of possibility (imkān), then 'fī' means 'in the presence of' (ʿinda), indicating a mental faculty; if taṣawwur is taken in the sense of actual perception (idrāk), then 'fī' means 'by means of' (bā’ al-sababiyya), indicating the instrument of perception. This precision guards against misunderstanding the relationship between the judgment and the mind.

Key Terms
واجب
مستحيل
جائز
تصور
وجوب
استحالة
جواز
إمكان
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definitions are circular or merely verbal.
Correction: They are precise logical formulations tied to mental conceivability, not empty verbal distinctions.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how is the possibility (jawāz) of a thing defined?
A) Non-conceivability of its non-existence in the mind.
B) Non-conceivability of its existence in the mind.
C) Validity of its existence and non-existence in the mind.
D) The actual existence of the thing in the external world.

Key Takeaway
The three rational classifications—necessary, impossible, and possible—are defined by what the mind can or cannot conceive, and the precision of their definitions is essential for sound theological reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is something you consider absolutely necessary for your happiness? Can you conceive of your life without it? If yes, then according to this lesson, it is not truly necessary (wājib) in the intellectual sense. How does this challenge your attachments?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God that you consider 'impossible' to be false. Is that impossibility based on mental conception or emotional certainty? How does Al-Bajuri's definition help you ground your theology in reason rather than feeling?
?
The possible (jā’iz) includes the validity of both existence and non-existence in the mind. Name one thing in your life that you treat as if it must exist (wājib) but is actually only possible. What would change if you mentally accepted its contingency?

The Reply That Tasawwur Means Tasdiq and the Two Types of Wajib
Sanusi replies to an objection by reinterpreting tasawwur as tasdiq and classifies wajib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'tasawwur' is reinterpreted as 'tasdiq' in the definition of wajib, and the distinction between necessary and theoretical wajib.

Core Idea
In kalam, 'tasawwur' can mean assent, not just conception.

This passage addresses a potential objection to the definition of wajib by clarifying the intended meaning of 'tasawwur'. Sanusi explains that 'tasawwur' here refers to 'tasdiq' (assent), which brings both necessary and theoretical obligations under the same definition. He then classifies wajib into two types with examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the reinterpretation
The passage redefines 'tasawwur' as 'tasdiq'. Why is this necessary? It allows the definition to cover both types of wajib.
3
Distinguish the two wajib types
Necessary wajib requires no inference (e.g., atom occupying space). Theoretical wajib requires inference (e.g., God's power). Note the examples.
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this classification applies to other attributes of Allah mentioned in the art.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب: بأن المراد بالتصور هنا التصديق، بمعنى: الإذعان والقبول. ودخل في التعريف كل من الواجب الضروري والواجب النظري، والأول: هو ما لا يحتاج إلى نظر واستدلال كالتحيز للجزم، بمعنى: أخذه قدراً من الفراغ الموهوم. والثاني: هو ما يحتاج إلى ذلك كقدرة الله تعالى، وكذا سائر ما ذكر في هذا الفن.
And it was answered: that what is meant by tasawwur (conception) here is tasdiq (assent), meaning: submission and acceptance. And included in the definition are both the necessary wajib (obligatory) and the theoretical wajib. The first is that which does not need consideration and inference, like existence-in-a-location for an atom (juz'), meaning: taking it as a quantity of imaginary space. The second is that which needs that, like the power of Allah, the Most High, and likewise the rest of what is mentioned in this art.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a classification. The author (or a commentator) first presents a response to an objection: the definition of wajib (obligatory) as that which reason cannot assent to its non-existence is criticized for only covering theoretical wajib (which requires inference). The answer is that 'tasawwur' (conception) in the definition is used to mean 'tasdiq' (assent), i.e., submission and acceptance. Thus the definition includes both necessary wajib, which is known without inference, and theoretical wajib, which requires inference. The necessary wajib is exemplified by the existence-in-a-location of an atom (juz'), which is taken as a quantity of imaginary space. The theoretical wajib is exemplified by the power of Allah. The passage then notes that all other items mentioned in this art similarly fall under these categories.

تصديق

واجب نظري
تحيز
الجزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'tasawwur' always means mere conception in kalam definitions.
Correction: In this context, 'tasawwur' is used terminologically to mean 'tasdiq' (assent), not mere conception.

Checkpoint

A) Mere conception (tasawwur haqiqi)
B) Assent and acceptance (tasdiq)
C) Consideration and inference (nazar wa istidlal)
D) Imaginary space (firagh mawhum)

Key Takeaway
The term 'tasawwur' in the definition of wajib is reinterpreted as 'tasdiq' (assent), allowing the definition to encompass both necessary and theoretical obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage reinterprets tasawwur as tasdiq, meaning submission and acceptance. In your daily life, do you merely conceive of Islamic teachings, or do you truly submit to them? Choose one command of Allah that you struggle with and commit to acting on it this week without first reasoning why it is beneficial.
?
Consider the theoretical wajib: God's power. You believe it based on inference, but how often do you live as if His power is a living reality that shapes your decisions? When you face a difficulty, do you turn to Him in reliance, or do you rely on your own reasoning first?

The Three Types of Wajib: Absolute, Restricted, and for Another
Al-Bajuri answers an objection and classifies wajib into absolute, restricted, and for another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of wajib (necessary) and how they apply to Allah and to created things, including the distinction between absolute and restricted necessity.

Core Idea
Wajib is not only absolute; it can be conditional or caused by another.

Al-Bajuri begins by addressing a potential confusion about how something that comes into being can be called wajib. He clarifies that wajib can be restricted by time or condition, and then expands to three distinct types: absolute, restricted, and for another. This lesson unpacks each type with examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Notice how the objection suggests that existence-in-a-location cannot be wajib because it is preceded and followed by non-existence. The answer introduces the concept of restricted wajib.
2
Identify the three types of wajib
Look for the three categories: absolute (Allah's essence and attributes), restricted (e.g., existence-in-a-location of an atom when it exists), and for another (e.g., a contingent whose existence is known by Allah).
Each has a different relation to necessity.
3
Examine the example of wajib li-ghayrihi
The example of a contingent whose existence is known by Allah shows how something possible in itself becomes necessary due to another factor—here, Allah's knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: كيف يكون تحيز الجزمِ واجباً مع أنه مسبوق بعَدَمٍ ويلحقه عَدَمٌ؟ لأنا نقول: المراد أنه واجب عند وجود الجزم، ولذلك يسمى واجباً مقيداً، وأما الواجب المطلق؛ فكذاته تعالى وصفاته وكلٌّ من هذين النوعين واجب لذاته. وهناك واجب لغيره؛ وإن كان جائزاً في ذاته، كوجود شيء من الممكنات في زَمَنِ عَلِمَ الله وجودَهُ فيه، فإنه - وإن كان ممكناً في ذاته - واجبٌ لتعلَّقِ عِلْمِ الله به. وهذه الأنواع تجري في المستحيل فالمستحيل الذاتي المطلق كالشريك، والمقيَّدُ كعَدَمِ تحيَّزِ الجِزم، والعرضي كوجود شيء من الممكنات في زمن عَلِمَ عَدَمَهُ فيه. فتدبر.
It is not to be said: How can existence-in-a-location for an atom be wajib while it is preceded by non-existence and followed by non-existence? For we say: What is meant is that it is wajib at the time of the existence of the atom, and therefore it is called restricted wajib. As for the absolute wajib, it is like His Essence, the Most High, and His attributes. And each of these two types is wajib in itself. And there is wajib for another, even if it is possible in itself, like the existence of something among possibles at a time when Allah knew its existence would be, for it—even though possible in itself—is wajib due to the attachment of Allah's knowledge to it. And these types apply to the mustahil (impossible), so the absolute essential impossible is like the partner (to Allah), and the restricted is like the non-existence of existence-in-a-location for an atom, and the accidental is like the existence of something among possibles at a time when Allah knew its non-existence. So reflect.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. The commentator (al-Bajuri) first addresses an anticipated objection: how can existence-in-a-location for an atom (juz', i.e., an indivisible particle) be considered wajib (necessary) when it is preceded and followed by non-existence? The answer is that it is wajib only when the atom exists, hence it is called 'restricted wajib' (wajib muqayyad). The absolute wajib (al-wajib al-mutlaq) is like Allah's Essence and attributes—these are wajib in themselves (wajib li-dhatihi). Then a third type is introduced: wajib for another (wajib li-ghayrihi), which is possible in itself but becomes necessary due to another factor, e.g., the existence of a contingent at a time Allah knew it would exist, because Allah's knowledge attaches to it, making it necessary. The same three types apply to the impossible (mustahil): the absolute essential impossible (like a partner to Allah), the restricted impossible (like the non-existence of existence-in-a-location for an atom), and the accidental impossible (like the existence of a contingent at a time Allah knew it would not exist). The passage concludes with 'fata'ammal' (so reflect).

Key Terms
واجب مطلق
واجب مقيد
واجب لغيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'wajib' always means absolutely necessary in itself, with no conditions or external causes.
Correction: This lesson shows that wajib can be restricted (conditional) or accidental (due to another), as in the example of a contingent whose existence is necessitated by Allah's knowledge.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of 'wajib li-ghayrihi' (necessary due to another)?
A) Allah's essence
B) Existence-in-a-location for an atom at the time of its existence
C) Existence of a contingent at a time Allah knew it would exist
D) Non-existence of existence-in-a-location for an atom

Key Takeaway
The three types of wajib—absolute, restricted, and for another—allow us to correctly describe necessity in creation and in Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence at this moment is necessary only because Allah knows it will be, how does that change the way you view your own choices and future? Will you live today with the certainty that Allah's knowledge encompasses every detail?
?
You are a possible being (mumkin) whose existence is made necessary by divine knowledge. What does that imply about your accountability to the One who knows you intimately? Does this realization give you more fear or more hope?
?
Recognizing that only Allah is absolutely necessary (wajib mutlaq), how does this affect your attachment to worldly things? Name one thing you are holding onto that is not absolutely necessary, and commit to releasing it today.

The Three Types of Mustahil: Absolute, Restricted, and Accidental
Al-Bajuri applies the threefold division to mustahil with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of impossibility (mustahil) and how they parallel the categories of necessity (wajib).

Core Idea
Impossibility mirrors necessity: it can be absolute, conditional, or accidental.

Al-Bajuri extends the same threefold classification to the impossible (mustahil). Just as necessity comes in absolute, restricted, and accidental forms, so too does impossibility. This lesson presents each type with clear examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of mustahil
Identify the three types: absolute essential impossible (e.g., partner for Allah), restricted impossible (e.g., non-existence of existence-in-a-location for an atom), and accidental impossible (e.g., existence of a contingent at a time Allah knew it wouldn't exist).
2
Compare with the three types of wajib
Notice the one-to-one correspondence: absolute wajib ↔ absolute impossible, restricted wajib ↔ restricted impossible, wajib li-ghayrihi ↔ accidental impossible.
3
Reflect on the examples
The examples highlight how impossibility can be inherent (partner for Allah), conditional (non-existence of something that exists), or accidental (a contingent that does not occur because Allah knew it wouldn't).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: كيف يكون تحيز الجزمِ واجباً مع أنه مسبوق بعَدَمٍ ويلحقه عَدَمٌ؟ لأنا نقول: المراد أنه واجب عند وجود الجزم، ولذلك يسمى واجباً مقيداً، وأما الواجب المطلق؛ فكذاته تعالى وصفاته وكلٌّ من هذين النوعين واجب لذاته. وهناك واجب لغيره؛ وإن كان جائزاً في ذاته، كوجود شيء من الممكنات في زَمَنِ عَلِمَ الله وجودَهُ فيه، فإنه - وإن كان ممكناً في ذاته - واجبٌ لتعلَّقِ عِلْمِ الله به. وهذه الأنواع تجري في المستحيل فالمستحيل الذاتي المطلق كالشريك، والمقيَّدُ كعَدَمِ تحيَّزِ الجِزم، والعرضي كوجود شيء من الممكنات في زمن عَلِمَ عَدَمَهُ فيه. فتدبر.
It is not to be said: How can existence-in-a-location for an atom be wajib while it is preceded by non-existence and followed by non-existence? This passage is a classification and proof. The commentator (al-Bajuri) first addresses an anticipated objection: how can existence-in-a-location for an atom (juz', i.e., an indivisible particle) be considered wajib (necessary) when it is preceded and followed by non-existence? The answer is that it is wajib only when the atom exists, hence it is called 'restricted wajib' (wajib muqayyad).
