Why 'in the mind'? Defending the Objectivity of the Three Categories
Al-Bajuri interprets the definite article in 'al-'aql' and addresses the Mu'tazila objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of the three rational judgments includes the phrase 'in the mind' and how al-Bajuri defends its objectivity against the Mu'tazila objection.

Core Idea
The definition's reference to the mind does not make the categories subjective.

Al-Bajuri now turns to a subtle point in Sanusi's wording: why does the definition say 'in the mind'? Does that mean the categories depend on the mind for their existence? This could open the door to relativism. In this passage, al-Bajuri explains two ways to understand the phrase and shows that the definition remains objective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Read the passage and notice that al-Bajuri is commenting on the phrase 'fi al-'aql' (in the mind) in the definition of wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz. Ask yourself why this phrase might be controversial.
The controversy is that it might imply the judgments are mind-dependent.
2
Understand the first interpretation
Al-Bajuri suggests that the definite article 'al' in 'al-'aql' could refer to a specific perfect individual intellect. This preserves objectivity because it points to a standard intellect.
3
Understand the second interpretation
Alternatively, 'al' could be for generality, but then we must disregard corrupting influences like the doubts of deviant sects. This also keeps the definition objective.
This interpretation repels the Mu'tazila objection directly.
4
Consider the residual objection
After repelling the Mu'tazila objection, al-Bajuri raises a further objection: the three categories are objective in themselves, so why link them to the mind at all? Notice that the passage ends without a full answer.
This shows that the commentary anticipates further discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله: في العقل يحتمل أن (أل) فيه للعهد والمعهود: الفرد الكامل، ويحتمل أنها للاستغراق، وعليه فهو شامل لكلّ عقل، لكن بقطع النظر عن العلائق ذلك، كالشُّبَهِ التي تقوم بعقل الفِرَق الضالة، فاندفع بذلك ما قد يقال: إنه قد يتصوَّرُ في بعض العقول عَدَمُ بعض الواجبات كعقل المعتزلة، فإنه قد تُصوّر فيه عدم القدرة ونحوها من صفات المعاني. المانعة من نعم؛ يَرِدُ أن الواجب واجبٌ في نفسه وُجِدَ عقل أو لم يوجد، وكذا المستحيل والجائز، فكان الأولى أن لا يَرْبطَ تعريف الثلاثة بالعقل
And His saying: 'in the mind' (fi al-'aql) - it is possible that the definite article (al) in it is for the previously mentioned, and what is previously mentioned is the perfect individual, and it is possible that it is for generality, and according to that it includes every mind, but disregarding those attachments, like the doubts that arise in the minds of deviant sects. Thus by that is repelled what might be said: that in some minds there may occur the non-existence of some necessary things, like the mind of the Mu'tazila, for there occurs in it the non-existence of power and similar attributes of meanings. The preventer from [that]... yes; it is objected that the wajib is wajib in itself whether a mind exists or not, and likewise the impossible and the possible, so it would have been better not to link the definition of the three to the mind.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'in the mind' in the definition of the three rational judgments (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz). The commentator (al-Bajuri) offers two interpretations of the definite article 'al' in 'al-'aql': either it refers to a specific perfect intellect (al-fard al-kamil), or it is generic, including every mind but disregarding corrupting influences like the doubts of deviant sects. This second interpretation repels the objection that in some minds (e.g., the Mu'tazila) one might conceive of the non-existence of some wajib things like divine attributes (they deny attributes). Then an objection is raised: the three categories are objective, existing irrespective of minds, so it would be better not to link their definition to the mind. The commentary presents this objection but does not fully resolve it; the phrase 'al-mani'ah min na'am' is obscure.

Key Terms
العقل
الفرد الكامل
الاستغراق
الشبه
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the definition of the three rational judgments is subjective because it mentions the mind.
Correction: Al-Bajuri shows that the mention of 'the mind' either refers to a perfect standard intellect or to every mind but disregarding corrupting influences, thus preserving objectivity.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator reconcile the definition of the three categories with the Mu'tazila's conception of non-existence of attributes?
A) By saying the Mu'tazila are not considered rational
B) By interpreting 'al-'aql' as the perfect individual intellect or generically but disregarding deviant doubts
C) By changing the definition to not mention the mind
D) By acknowledging that the Mu'tazila's conception is valid

Key Takeaway
The definition's mention of the mind does not compromise objectivity because it either refers to a perfect intellect or to every mind stripped of corrupting influences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's intellectual objection as mere ignorance, like the Mu'tazila's doubts, without first seeking to understand their reasoning? How does al-Bajuri's careful approach of distinguishing a perfect intellect from corrupted minds challenge you to handle objections with patience and nuance? What specific step will you take this week to listen charitably to a viewpoint you initially reject?
?
The source emphasizes that the necessary truths about Allah are objective, independent of any mind. Is there an attribute of Allah you have been treating as negotiable or dependent on your feelings? Confess that to yourself and commit to affirming His perfection regardless of your emotional state.
?
Al-Bajuri includes the phrase 'in the mind' in the definition despite the objection that it might imply subjectivity. When you explain your faith to others, do you avoid difficult clarifications to keep things simple, or do you patiently address apparent ambiguities like this? Recall a recent conversation where you could have clarified a misconception about Allah but didn't. How will you handle a similar opportunity in the future?

Types of Necessary and Impossible
Al-Bajuri classifies wajib and mustahil into three types each.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of necessary (wajib) and impossible (mustahil) as classified by al-Bajuri, and how they apply to theological examples.

Core Idea
Necessity and impossibility come in absolute, restricted, and accidental forms.

Al-Bajuri deepens our understanding of rational judgments by showing that necessary and impossible are not monolithic. Some things are necessary only under a condition, others are necessary in themselves, and yet others are necessary because of something external. This classification applies symmetrically to impossibility. In this lesson, we will unpack these distinctions and see how they clarify theological statements about God and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how al-Bajuri begins with an objection and then clarifies.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three types of necessary
Look for 'wajib mutlaq', 'wajib muqayyad', and 'wajib li-ghayrihi'. Understand the examples: God's essence vs. atom's location vs. a future event known by God.
Each type has a distinct logical status.
3
Map the same classification to impossible
Find 'mustahil dhati', 'mustahil muqayyad', and 'mustahil 'aradi'. The examples are 'partner for God', 'atom not occupying space', and 'event known not to happen'.
The symmetry is precise.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself why this classification matters. It prevents confusion about what 'necessary' means in different contexts, especially when discussing God's attributes and creation.
This is a foundational distinction in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال: كيف يكون تحيز الجزمِ واجباً مع أنه مسبوق بعَدَمٍ ويلحقه عَدَمٌ؟ لأنا نقول: المراد أنه واجب عند وجود الجزم، ولذلك يسمى واجباً مقيداً، وأما الواجب المطلق؛ فكذاته تعالى وصفاته وكلٌّ من هذين النوعين واجب لذاته. وهناك واجب لغيره؛ وإن كان جائزاً في ذاته، كوجود شيء من الممكنات في زَمَنِ عَلِمَ الله وجودَهُ فيه، فإنه - وإن كان ممكناً في ذاته - واجبٌ لتعلَّقِ عِلْمِ الله به. وهذه الأنواع تجري في المستحيل فالمستحيل الذاتي المطلق كالشريك، والمقيَّدُ كعَدَمِ تحيَّزِ الجِزم، والعرضي كوجود شيء من الممكنات في زمن عَلِمَ عَدَمَهُ فيه. فتدبر.
It is not said: How is the occupation of place (tahayyuz) of the atom (al-jazm) necessary (wajib) while it is preceded by non-existence and followed by non-existence? We say: The intended meaning is that it is necessary at the time of the existence of the atom, and therefore it is called restricted necessary (wajib muqayyad). As for the absolute necessary (al-wajib al-mutlaq), it is like His essence, exalted, and His attributes. Each of these two types is necessary in itself (wajib li-dhatihi). And there is necessary for other than itself (wajib li-ghayrihi), even if it is possible (ja'iz) in itself, like the existence of something from among possible things in a time in which God knew its existence in it. For it—even if it is possible in itself—is necessary due to God's knowledge being attached to it. These types also apply to the impossible (al-mustahil). So the absolute essential impossible (al-mustahil al-dhati al-mutlaq) is like the partner (al-sharik); the restricted impossible (al-muqayyad) is like non-occupation of place of the atom; and the accidental impossible (al-'aradi) is like the existence of something from among possible things in a time in which He knew its non-existence in it. So ponder.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of necessary (wajib) and impossible (mustahil). It begins by addressing an objection: how can the occupation of place (tahayyuz) of the atom (al-jazm) be necessary when it is preceded by non-existence and followed by non-existence? The response clarifies that it is necessary in a restricted sense: it is necessary at the time of its existence, thus called 'restricted necessary' (wajib muqayyad). This is contrasted with 'absolute necessary' (wajib mutlaq) which is necessary in itself, like God's essence and attributes. Both of these are necessary in themselves (wajib li-dhatihi). Then the author introduces a third type: 'necessary for another' (wajib li-ghayrihi), which is possible in itself but becomes necessary due to an external factor, such as God's knowledge attaching to its existence in a specific time. The same classification applies to the impossible (mustahil): absolute essential impossible (mustahil dhati mutlaq) like the partner (sharik) with God; restricted impossible (mustahil muqayyad) like the lack of occupation of place for the atom; and accidental impossible (mustahil 'aradi) like the existence of something in a time when God knew it would not exist. The passage concludes with 'ponder' (tadabbar), urging reflection on this classification.

Key Terms
واجب
مستحيل
جائز
واجب مقيد
واجب مطلق
واجب لذاته
واجب لغيره
مستحيل ذاتي
مستحيل عرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Something preceded by non-existence cannot be truly necessary.
Correction: The passage shows that conditional necessity (wajib muqayyad) is legitimate; it is necessary at the time of its existence, even if not eternal.

Checkpoint
Which type of impossible is exemplified by 'a partner with God'?
A) Mustahil muqayyad
B) Mustahil 'aradi
C) Mustahil dhati mutlaq
D) Mustahil dhati muqayyad

Key Takeaway
Necessity and impossibility are not uniform; they divide into absolute, restricted, and accidental types, each with distinct logical grounds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence is only 'restricted necessary'—dependent on God's will at each moment—does your heart truly feel that dependence, or do you live as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you internalized that you are not 'absolute necessary'?
?
When you face something you consider impossible, does the lesson's distinction between essential and accidental impossibility encourage you to examine whether your assessment is based on God's knowledge or your own limitation?
?
The passage says something can be 'possible in itself' but 'necessary for another' due to God's knowledge. What does that mean for how you view your own plans? Do you accept that what you hope for is only possible while God knows it will happen? Can you surrender even your 'possibilities' to His decree?

Objection and Response on Defining Judgments by the Intellect
Al-Bajuri presents an objection and response on defining judgments by the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definitions of rational judgments must include reference to the intellect, and how Al-Bajuri rebuts the objection that they should not.

Core Idea
Rational judgments are defined from the intellect's perspective, not absolute reality.

This passage addresses a subtle methodological objection: if necessary, impossible, and possible are what they are independent of any intellect, why define them as 'rational necessary', etc.? Al-Bajuri presents the objection and then provides a precise rebuttal, clarifying that the definitions pertain to judgments as perceived by the intellect, not to the realities in themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand why someone might think the intellect is irrelevant: the necessary, impossible, and possible are what they are in themselves regardless of any intellect.
Note that the objection cites definitions from 'al-Maqasid' and 'al-Mawaqif' that omit the intellect.
2
Read the rebuttal
See how Al-Bajuri distinguishes between rational judgments (defined from the intellect's perspective) and actual judgments (absolute realities).
The rebuttal states that what is being defined is the rational necessary, etc.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider the difference between 'rational necessary' and 'actual necessary' and why this distinction is crucial for the methodology of kalam.
Alternative interpretations are also offered: the restriction may be implied in other works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نعم؛ يَرِدُ أن الواجب واجبٌ في نفسه وُجِدَ عقل أو لم يوجد، وكذا المستحيل والجائز، فكان الأولى أن لا يَرْبطَ تعريف الثلاثة بالعقل، كأن يقول: لتكلُّفَاتٍ ذَكَرَها ، وليوافِقَ قول "المقاصد" و"المواقف": الواجب: ما لا يُمْكِنُ عدمُهُ، ولأن الواجب واجب، والمستحيل مستحيل، والممكن ممكن في نفس الأمر، وُجِدَ عقل أم لا. وتبعه على ذلك أرباب الحواشي، لكنه قرَّرَهُ قائلاً: أقول ذلك مع الوَجَلِ، وتأمل فيه ليظهر لك ما فيه اهـ. ولك دَفْعُهُ : بأن المعرَّفَ: الواجبُ العقلي والمستحيل العقلي والجائز العقلي، فلا بُدَّ من اعتبار العقل في التعريف فالمقصود للمصنف تعريفها من حيث إدراك العقل، لا من حيث صفته الواقعية مجرَّدَةً عن إدراك العقل، وأما تعريف صاحب "المقاصد" و"المواقف"؛ فباعتبار الوجوب الواقعي، أو يقال: القيد ملحوظ فيه أيضاً.
Yes; it is objected that the necessary is necessary in itself whether an intellect exists or not, and likewise the impossible and the possible. So it would be better not to tie the definition of the three to the intellect, like saying: due to the difficulties he mentioned, and to agree with the statement of 'al-Maqasid' and 'al-Mawaqif': the necessary is that which cannot be non-existent, and because the necessary is necessary, the impossible is impossible, and the possible is possible in actual fact, whether an intellect exists or not. The authors of the glosses followed him in that, but he stated it saying: I say that with fear, and reflect on it so that what is in it becomes clear to you. End. And you can rebut it: by saying that what is defined is the rational necessary, the rational impossible, and the rational possible, so it is necessary to consider the intellect in the definition. For the author's intention is to define them from the perspective of the intellect's perception, not from their actual attribute stripped of intellect's perception. As for the definition of the author of 'al-Maqasid' and 'al-Mawaqif', it is by consideration of actual necessity, or it is said: the restriction is also considered in it.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its rebuttal regarding the inclusion of the phrase 'in the intellect' (fi al-aql) in the definitions of the three judgments. The objection states that necessary, impossible, and possible are what they are in themselves regardless of whether any intellect exists or not. Therefore, it would be better not to tie the definitions to the intellect. The objector suggests agreeing with the definitions given in 'al-Maqasid' and 'al-Mawaqif', which define the necessary as that which cannot be non-existent, etc., without mentioning the intellect. The author of the hashya then presents the rebuttal: what is being defined here is not the absolute judgments but rather the rational judgments (al-wajib al-'aqli, etc.). The definition is from the perspective of the intellect's perception, not from their real essence independent of the intellect. The definitions in 'al-Maqasid' and 'al-Mawaqif' are by consideration of actual necessity, so they are different. Alternatively, it is said that the restriction (i.e., 'in the intellect') is also implied in those definitions. Thus, the inclusion of 'fi al-aql' is justified and necessary for the purpose of defining rational judgments.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Including 'intellect' in the definitions makes them subjective or dependent on human reason for their truth.
Correction: The definitions refer to judgments as perceived by the intellect, not the absolute realities themselves. The judgments themselves are objective; the intellect merely recognizes them.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The inclusion of 'intellect' in the definitions of rational judgments is necessary because they are defined from the intellect's perspective, not as absolute realities.

The objector thought that categories are independent of the intellect. What concrete step will you take this week to humble your intellect and seek God's guidance through the revealed sources?

The Three Rational Judgments: Wajib, Mustahil, and Ja'iz
Sanusi defines the three rational judgments and addresses the role of intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) and why Sanusi's definitions rely on intellectual perception.

Core Idea
The three rational judgments are defined by what the intellect perceives as possible.

Sanusi begins by laying the foundation for theological reasoning: the three categories of rational judgment. These are not just abstract concepts but the very grammar of kalam. In this passage, we encounter his definitions and an important discussion about whether these definitions depend on the existence of the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the concise wording of each definition.
Sanusi uses: 'does not accept non-existence' for necessary, etc.
2
Understand the objection
Some scholars proposed absolute definitions that ignore the intellect. Identify the alternative definitions and why they were proposed.
The alternative comes from al-Maqasid and al-Mawaqif.
3
Learn the defense
The commentator defends Sanusi by clarifying that the definitions are about intellectual judgments, not absolute reality.
He says the defined terms are 'intellectual necessary' etc.
4
Reflect on the role of the intellect
Consider why this distinction matters for kalam. The intellect is necessary for rational judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواجبُ ما لا يَقْبَلُ الانتفاء، والمستحيل : ما لا يقبلُ الثبوت، والجائز: ما يَقْبَلُهما. لتكلُّفَاتٍ ذَكَرَها ، وليوافِقَ قول "المقاصد" و"المواقف": الواجب: ما لا يُمْكِنُ عدمُهُ، ولأن الواجب واجب، والمستحيل مستحيل، والممكن ممكن في نفس الأمر، وُجِدَ عقل أم لا. وتبعه على ذلك أرباب الحواشي، لكنه قرَّرَهُ قائلاً: أقول ذلك مع الوَجَلِ، وتأمل فيه ليظهر لك ما فيه اهـ. ولك دَفْعُهُ : بأن المعرَّفَ: الواجبُ العقلي والمستحيل العقلي والجائز العقلي، فلا بُدَّ من اعتبار العقل في التعريف فالمقصود للمصنف تعريفها من حيث إدراك العقل، لا من حيث صفته الواقعية مجرَّدَةً عن إدراك العقل، وأما تعريف صاحب "المقاصد" و"المواقف"؛ فباعتبار الوجوب الواقعي، أو يقال: القيد ملحوظ فيه أيضاً.
The necessary is that which does not accept non-existence, the impossible is that which does not accept existence, and the possible is that which accepts both. On account of some affectations he mentioned, and to agree with the statement of al-Maqasid and al-Mawaqif: the necessary is that whose non-existence is not possible, and because the necessary is necessary, the impossible is impossible, and the possible is possible in itself, whether an intellect exists or not. And the masters of the glosses followed him in that, but he stated it saying: I say that with reverence, and reflect on it so that what is in it becomes clear to you. And you can refute it by saying: the defined is the intellectual necessary, the intellectual impossible, and the intellectual possible, so considering the intellect in the definition is necessary. What the author meant is to define them in terms of the intellect's perception, not in terms of their real attribute stripped of intellect's perception. As for the definition of the author of al-Maqasid and al-Mawaqif, it is by considering real necessity, or it is said: the condition is also considered in it.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines the three rational judgments and defends the author's definition against an objection. It begins with Sanusi's definitions: the necessary is that which does not accept non-existence, the impossible is that which does not accept existence, and the possible is that which accepts both. Then the commentator (al-Anbabi) notes that some scholars, to avoid affectations and to agree with other works like al-Maqasid and al-Mawaqif, defined these terms in an absolute sense: the necessary is that whose non-existence is not possible, the impossible and possible are as they are in themselves, regardless of the existence of an intellect. The commentator then says that the masters of the glosses followed this absolute definition, but he himself expresses reverence and invites reflection. He provides a defense: the defined terms in Sanusi's definition are the intellectual necessary, intellectual impossible, and intellectual possible, so the intellect must be considered. The author's purpose is to define them from the perspective of intellectual perception, not from their real attributes stripped of intellectual perception. The definitions in al-Maqasid and al-Mawaqif consider real necessity, but even there the condition might be implicitly considered.

Correction: Sanusi's definitions are specifically about intellectual judgments—the intellect's perception of necessity, impossibility, and possibility.

Pronoun Reference in the Definition of the Necessary
Al-Bajuri clarifies pronoun reference; al-Ajhuri answers a question about perceiving absence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the pronoun in 'adamuhu' (its non-existence) in the definition of the necessary (wajib) refers to individual instances and not the universal concept, and how this resolves a potential contradiction about conceiving the absence of the necessary.

Core Idea
The pronoun in 'adamuhu' refers to individual attributes, not the universal concept.

In theological definitions, every word matters. The pronoun in 'adamuhu' (its non-existence) in Sanusi's definition of the necessary (wajib) might seem trivial, but it actually prevents a serious misunderstanding. Al-Bajuri clarifies that it refers to specific individual instances like power and will, not the universal concept of 'that which is necessary'. Then al-Ajhuri shows how this nuance answers a question: how can we say the necessary cannot be conceived as non-existent when we can think of its essence as absent from our minds when we are heedless? Let's explore this carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the complete Arabic passage from al-Bajuri and al-Ajhuri once, then read the literal English translation. Notice the two distinct parts: first the pronoun clarification by al-Bajuri, then al-Ajhuri's question-and-answer.
Pay attention to the phrase 'باعتبار الأفراد' (considering individuals) – this is key.
2
Understand the pronoun reference issue
Al-Bajuri states that the pronoun in 'adamuhu' goes back to 'ma' (that which) but specifically considering individual instances (afrad) like power and will, not the universal concept (mafhum kulli). Why does this distinction matter? Think about what happens if we take it as the universal concept.
The universal concept of 'the necessary' can be absent from the mind, but individual instances of necessity (like God's power) cannot be absent.
3
Analyze al-Ajhuri's question and answer
Al-Ajhuri presents a hypothetical objection: if the necessary is that which cannot be conceived as absent, yet a person can conceive of the essence of the necessary as absent when they are unaware of it. The answer hinges on the pronoun reference: the definition applies to the actual necessary, not the mental concept. The heedless person does not judge anything; another person judges the absence from the heedless one's mind.
The key: the one who judges the absence is different from the one who is heedless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حاشية البيجوري على السنوسية (قوله: عَدَمُهُ) الضمير عائد على ( ما ) باعتبار الأفراد كالقدرة والإرادة لا باعتبار المفهوم الكلي كما هو ظاهر. حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري (قوله: الضَّمِيرُ عَائِدٌ عَلَى مَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الْأَفْرَادِ... إلخ) جواب عن سؤال، حاصله: أن المصنف قد حكم على الواجب بأنه لا يتصوَّر في العقل عدمه مع أن حقيقة الواجب قد يتصور في العقل عدمها عند الغفلة عنها وعدم استحضارها في الذهن، بأن لم يُخْطِرْ بالبال هذا التعريف الذي ذكره المصنف، فحينئذ يصدق العقل بأن حقيقة الواجب معدومة، أي: ليست حاضرة في ذهن الشخص الغافل عنها، والمصدق بعدمها شخص آخر غير هذا الغافل.
Annotation of al-Bajuri on al-Sanussiyya: (His saying: 'its non-existence') the pronoun refers to 'what' (ma) considering individuals like power and will, not considering the universal concept as is apparent. Annotation of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri: (His saying: 'the pronoun refers to 'what' considering individuals... etc.') is the answer to a question, the summary of which is: The author judged that the necessary is not conceivable in the mind as non-existent, whereas the essence of the necessary can be conceived in the mind as non-existent when one is heedless of it and does not bring it to mind, by not having this definition mentioned by the author in mind. Then the mind would truthfully say that the essence of the necessary is non-existent, i.e., it is not present in the mind of the heedless person, and the one who affirms its non-existence is another person other than this heedless one.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage resolves a pronoun ambiguity and answers a potential question. First, al-Bajuri clarifies that in the definition of wajib ('that which does not accept non-existence'), the pronoun in 'adamuhu (its non-existence) refers to 'ma' (that which) considering individual instances (afrad) like power and will, not the universal concept (mafhum kulli). This is important because the universal concept may or may not exist in the mind. Then al-Ajhuri presents a question: If the necessary is defined as that which cannot be conceived as non-existent, yet one can conceive of the essence of the necessary as non-existent when they are not paying attention to it and not bringing this definition to mind—for example, if a person is unaware, their mind might judge that the essence of the necessary is absent from their mind. The answer is that the pronoun refers to individual instances, not the universal concept. The person who judges the essence of the necessary as non-existent is a different person from the one who is heedless; the heedless person does not judge anything about it. The definition applies to the necessary itself, not to its mental representation when forgotten.

Key Terms
الضمير
الأفراد
المفهوم الكلي
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the pronoun in 'adamuhu' refers to the universal concept of the necessary (mafhum kulli), leading to the objection that the necessary can be conceived as non-existent when one is unaware of it.
Correction: The pronoun actually refers to individual instances (afrad) like power and will, not the universal concept. The definition applies to the necessary itself, not to its mental representation. The person who judges the absence is a different person from the one who is heedless.

Checkpoint
To what does the pronoun in 'adamuhu' in the definition of wajib refer according to al-Bajuri?
A) The universal concept of wajib
B) The individual instances of wajib like power and will
C) The definition itself
D) The essence of the necessary

Key Takeaway
The pronoun in 'adamuhu' refers to individual instances, not the universal concept, and this resolves the apparent contradiction of conceiving the necessary as absent when heedless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The pronoun in 'its non-existence' refers to individual attributes like power and will. Which of Allah's attributes do you most often forget when you are distracted? Will you commit today to remembering at least one attribute each time you catch your mind wandering?
?
Al-Ajhuri's answer saves the definition of the Necessary from seeming contradiction. Are there aspects of your faith that you have doubted because you only looked at a partial picture? What can you do to ensure you see the complete logical and theological grounding of your beliefs before dismissing them?

Resolving the Pronoun in the Definition of the Necessary
Sanusi's commentators explain the pronoun in 'adamuhu' and refute an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complex debate surrounding the pronoun in Sanusi's definition of the Necessary, and how the commentators resolve an apparent objection about the conceivability of non-existence.

Core Idea
The pronoun in 'its non-existence' refers to individuals, not the universal — or to the essence itself.

Sanusi's definition of the Necessary as 'an entity whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind' seems vulnerable to an objection: if the pronoun refers to the universal concept, it can be absent from the mind. In this passage, the commentators dissect the definition, offering multiple defenses and ultimately clarifying the intended reference. Their discussion reveals the precision required in kalam definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Understand the objection that the universal concept of 'what' can be mentally absent, and the first answer that 'its' refers to individuals like power and will.
The initial response shifts the reference from the universal to its instances.
2
Examine the three additional defenses
Learn the three points: (1) Sanusi did not rule on the composite; (2) 'Non-existence' means external, not mental; (3) The definition is comprehensive and exclusive.
These defenses reinforce the definition's validity from different angles.
3
Understand the counter-response
Note that the first answer leads to absurdity in statements like 'God's knowledge is necessary', because it forces an incorrect meaning.
Al-Anbabi points out the logical flaw in the initial answer.
4
Grasp the final resolution
The true solution is that there is no objection: the pronoun refers to 'what' itself, i.e., the essence of the Necessary.
This preserves the definition without causing logical issues.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: عَدَمُهُ) الضمير عائد على ( ما ) باعتبار الأفراد كالقدرة والإرادة لا باعتبار المفهوم الكلي كما هو ظاهر.
وحاصل الجواب أن الضمير في قوله : (عدمه) عائد على (ما) باعتبار الأفراد، فالذي لا يصدق العقل بعدمه هو أفراد الواجب لا حقيقته، وهذا السؤال لا يرد إلا لو قال المصنف فالواجب لا يتصوّر في العقل عدمه، وهو لم يقل ذلك، وإنما عرف الواجب أن حقيقته شيء لا يتصور في العقل عدمه، فجعل عدم تصور العدم جزءاً من حقيقة الواجب هو فصلها المميز لها عن حقيقة المستحيل والجائز، ولم يحكم على تلك الحقيقة المركبة من الجنس المعبر عنه بـ (ما) والفصل المعبر عنه بقوله: (لا يتصور في العقل عدمه) بأنها لا يتصور في العقل عدمها، وإنما جعل عدم تصور العدم جزءاً لها مميزاً لها عن غيرها، فلا وجه لهذا الإيراد. وأيضاً قول المصنف: (عدمه) أراد به العدم الخارجي، فلا يرد عليه ما يتصور عدمه ذهناً وإنما يرد عليه ما يتصور عدمه خارجاً. وأيضاً فمدار التعريف على أن يكون جامعاً لأفراد المعرف مانعاً من دخول غيرها، وهذا التعريف كذلك.
فتحصل أن هذا الإيراد ممنوع من جهات ثلاث. وما أجاب به عنه من أن الضمير عائد على ( ما ) باعتبار الأفراد يصير عليه معنى المتن الواجب أمر لا يتصور في العقل عدم أفراده، وهذا يؤدي إلى بطلان الإخبار في قولنا: علم الله واجب؛ إذ يصير المعنى عليه : علم الله أمر لا يتصور في العقل عدم أفراده، وهذا المعنى غير صحيح فالحق أن لا إيراد ولا جواب بل الضمير عائد على (ما) باعتبار نفسها.
His words: 'its non-existence'—the pronoun refers to 'what' (ma) in terms of individuals, like power and will, not in terms of the universal concept, as is apparent. The summary of the answer is that the pronoun in his words 'its non-existence' refers to 'what' in terms of individuals, so what the mind does not affirm non-existence of is the individuals of the Necessary, not its essence. This objection would only arise if the author had said 'the Necessary is such that its non-existence is not conceivable in the mind', but he did not say that. Rather, he defined the Necessary as 'an entity whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind', making the inconceivability of non-existence a part of the essence of the Necessary, its differentia distinguishing it from the essence of the Impossible and the Possible. He did not rule that this composite essence—composed of the genus expressed by 'what' and the differentia expressed by 'whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind'—is such that its non-existence is not conceivable in the mind. Rather, he made the inconceivability of non-existence a part of it distinguishing it from others, so there is no basis for this objection. Also, the author's words 'its non-existence' intended external non-existence, so mental non-existence is not relevant; only external non-existence would be relevant. Also, the definition's purpose is to encompass all individuals of the defined and exclude others, and this definition does so. Hence it is established that this objection is refutable from three aspects. And what he answered with—that the pronoun refers to 'what' in terms of individuals—makes the meaning of the text 'the Necessary is an entity whose non-existence of its individuals is not conceivable in the mind', which leads to the invalidity of the predication in our statement 'God's knowledge is necessary'; because the meaning would then be 'God's knowledge is an entity whose non-existence of its individuals is not conceivable in the mind', and this meaning is incorrect. So the truth is that there is neither objection nor answer; rather, the pronoun refers to 'what' in terms of itself.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer sequence concerning the definition of the Necessary (al-wajib) in Sanusi's Umm al-Barahin. The objection argues that if 'its non-existence' in the definition refers to the universal concept of the Necessary, then that universal can be absent from the mind (e.g., when one is heedless), making the definition invalid. The first response (by al-Ajhuri) explains that the pronoun 'its' refers to the individuals (afrad) of the Necessary, not the universal concept. For example, specific attributes like power and will are individuals of the Necessary; their non-existence is inconceivable. The universal concept itself is not necessary—it can be present or absent in the mind. Al-Ajhuri then provides three additional defenses: (1) The author did not state that the Necessary itself has inconceivable non-existence; rather, he made that inconceivability a part (differentia) of the definition, not a ruling on the composite. (2) 'Non-existence' in the definition means external non-existence, not mental absence. (3) The definition is intended to be comprehensive and exclusive, which it is. Finally, al-Anbabi comments that this answer is problematic because it leads to absurdity in statements like 'God's knowledge is necessary'—since knowledge is an individual, the definition would require that its individuals (which are not applicable) have inconceivable non-existence. The true resolution, he says, is that there is no objection to begin with: the pronoun refers to 'what' (the essence) itself, not to individuals.

Key Terms
الضمير
المفهوم الكلي
الأفراد
العدم الخارجي
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pronoun in 'its non-existence' refers to the universal concept of 'entity' (al-mahiyya al-kulliyya).
Correction: The correct reference is to the individuals (afrad) of that universal, or ultimately to the essence itself.

Checkpoint
According to the commentators, why does the objection to the definition of the Necessary fail when considering the pronoun's reference?
A) The pronoun refers to the universal concept, which is always present in the mind.
B) The pronoun refers to individuals, whose non-existence is inconceivable externally.
C) The pronoun refers to the essence itself, which is not subject to mental absence.
D) The objection is invalid because the author's wording is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The pronoun in Sanusi's definition is most properly understood as referring to the essence itself, avoiding both the original objection and the pitfalls of alternative readings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think about God's necessity, do you ever stop to examine the exact words and pronouns you use, or do you settle for a vague understanding that might contain hidden flaws? Are you willing to go through the discomfort of logical analysis to ensure your belief is precise?
?
The scholars in this passage corrected a subtle mistake in interpreting a pronoun. Is there any area of your faith where you have accepted a superficial answer rather than digging into the details? What would it take for you to pursue the truth with that same rigor?
?
Al-Anbabi showed that even a well-intentioned answer can lead to absurdity when applied to God's attributes. Have you ever held a belief about God that, when pressed, led to an implication you didn't intend? Are you ready to revise it in light of deeper reflection?

The Derivation of 'Mustahil': Request or Mutawa'a?
Commentators analyze the derivation of 'mustahil' and argue for the mutawa'a interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the lexical debate over the derivation of 'mustahil' (the impossible) and why the mutawa'a interpretation is preferred in the kalam tradition.

Core Idea
The word 'mustahil' is not about seeking impossibility but about becoming impossible.

In classical kalam, technical terms often carry precise morphological implications. The word 'mustahil' (impossible) is derived from the verb 'istahala', which contains the prefix 'ista'. Commentators debate whether this prefix indicates request (talab) or mutawa'a (acquiescence). This lesson unpacks that debate and its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice that the passage critiques one view before presenting the preferred one.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage presents two possible meanings for the sin and ta in 'mustahil': request (talab) and mutawa'a. Find where each is introduced and see how they are supported or weakened.
The request view is associated with 'the obligated person deeming something impossible'.
3
Understand the arguments against the request interpretation
Two arguments are given: first, the word is used for things like 'partner' without regard to request; second, 'istahala' is not transitive like 'istaghfara'. Understand why these weaken the request view.
The second argument about transitivity is technical—focus on the main point.
4
Grasp the mutawa'a interpretation and its support
The mutawa'a interpretation means 'to become impossible' as a result of an external act. Support comes from al-Tashil and al-Qamus, showing that 'istaf'ala' can be mutawa'a for 'af'ala'. The meaning then relates to God's speech indicating impossibility.
This interpretation ties the term to divine action rather than human seeking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: (وَالْمُسْتَحِيلُ) قيل: السينُ والتاء فيه للطلب بمعنى: أنه طلب من المكلَّف أن يُحِيلَهُ - أي يعتقد أنه محال .. وضُعِفَ: بأن هذا اسم لنحو الشريك بقطع النظر عن الطلب، وهذا يُوهِمُ أنه منظورٌ للطلب في هذه التسمية، وليس كذلك.
وَرَدَّ بعضُ مشايخنا كَوْنَهما للطلب بأن استحال ليس متعدياً كاستغفرتُ الله .واستقدرتُه ورُدَّ كونُهما للتعدي بما ذكر أيضاً.
واختار بعضهم أنها للمطاوَعَةِ .
(قوله: وَاخْتَارَ بَعْضُهُمْ... إلخ) نصَّ في "التسهيل" على أن استفعل يكون مطاوعاً لأفعل، وفي "القاموس" : المُحَالُ من الكلام - بالضم - : ما عُدِلَ عن وجهه كالمستحيل اهـ. وقد تبين منه: أن الاستحالة في الأصل بمعنى: التقلب والانحراف، من: التحول، فمعنى أحاله حَرَفَهُ، فاستحال انْحَرَفَ. والمعنى على المطاوَعَةِ: أَحَلْتُهُ فاستحال، أي: اعْتَقَدْتُ مُحَالِيَّتَهُ فقَبِلَ ذلك الاعتقاد، وضع تعلقه به لكونه محالاً، أو المعنى: أحاله الله فاستحال. والمراد بإحالة الله : دلالته بكلامه القديم على أنه محال.
His words: 'and the impossible (al-mustahil)'—it is said: the sin and ta in it are for request (talab), meaning: it is requested from the obligated person that he deem it impossible (yuhilahu)—i.e., believe that it is impossible. And it was weakened (du'ifa) by: that this is a name for something like the partner (al-sharik) regardless of request, and this suggests that request is intended in this naming, but it is not so. Some of our teachers rejected that they are for request because 'istahala' is not transitive like 'istaghfartu Allah' and 'istaqdartuhu', and it was rejected that they are for transitivity by what was also mentioned. And some chose that they are for mutawa'a (acquiescence). His words: 'and some chose that they are for mutawa'a...'—it is stated in al-Tashil that 'istaf'ala' is mutawa'a for 'af'ala', and in al-Qamus: 'al-muhal (with damma) from speech: that which is turned from its proper course, like al-mustahil'. It becomes clear from this that istihala originally means turning and deviation, from tahawwul. So the meaning of 'ahalahu' is 'he turned it', so 'istahala' means 'it turned'. And the meaning according to mutawa'a: 'I deemed it impossible (ahaltuhu) so it became impossible (istahala)', i.e., I believed in its impossibility and it accepted that belief, and its relation to it is established because it is impossible. Or the meaning: God made it impossible (ahalahu) so it became impossible (istahala). And by 'God's making it impossible' is meant His indicating by His eternal speech that it is impossible.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a lexical and theological analysis of the word 'mustahil' (impossible) used in kalam. The commentators examine the morphology: the sin and ta (the prefix 'ista') can indicate either request (talab) or mutawa'a (acquiescence, i.e., the subject receives the action). The first opinion holds that 'mustahil' is an active participle meaning 'one who seeks to deem something impossible' (i.e., the believer). This is weakened because the word is used for entities like 'the partner' (sharik, i.e., associating partners with God) without regard to the act of requesting. A further objection is that the verb 'istahala' is not transitive like 'istaghfara', so it cannot be for request. The second opinion, chosen by some, is that the sin and ta are for mutawa'a, meaning 'to become impossible' as a result of an external act. This is supported by references to al-Tashil and al-Qamus, where 'istaf'ala' is mutawa'a for 'af'ala'. The original meaning of istihala is 'turning' or 'deviation'. Thus, 'ahalahu' means 'he turned it', and 'istahala' means 'it turned'. Applied to the theological context, either the believer deems something impossible and it becomes impossible (i.e., accepts that judgment), or God makes it impossible (by revealing it as such) and it becomes impossible. The latter interpretation emphasizes divine action: God's eternal speech indicates the impossibility of certain things, so the creature's knowledge follows.

Key Terms
المستحيل
السين والتاء
الطلب
المطاوعة
استحال
أحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The prefix 'ista' in Arabic always indicates a request or seeking, so 'mustahil' means 'that which is sought to be impossible'.
Correction: The prefix 'ista' can also indicate mutawa'a (acquiescence), where the subject undergoes the action. In 'mustahil', the mutawa'a interpretation means 'it becomes impossible' due to an external cause, which is more consistent with the theological usage.

Checkpoint
What is one of the arguments given against the request (talab) interpretation of 'mustahil'?
A) The word is not an active participle.
B) The verb 'istahala' is not transitive.
C) The word is used for entities like 'the partner' implying a request.
D) The sin and ta always indicate mutawa'a.

Key Takeaway
The passage demonstrates that technical kalam terms are carefully defined, and the derivation of 'mustahil' as 'becoming impossible' aligns with the idea that impossibility is a real attribute determined by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever approached a term in your faith with a superficial understanding, assuming you knew its meaning? This lesson reveals how a single prefix can change the entire theological implication. Will you commit to digging deeper into the precise meanings of Islamic terms, even when they seem familiar?
?
The passage shows that impossibility is not just a human judgment but rooted in God's eternal speech. What is one thing in your life that you have considered impossible to overcome, and how does this lesson challenge you to see it as part of God's determined reality? ?

Are there areas where you assume your own ability or inability is independent of God? This lesson calls you to acknowledge that all things, even perceived impossibilities, are under His control. Refutation of an Objection to the Definition of al-Wajib
Refutation of an objection to the definition of al-Wajib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection to Sanusi's definition of al-Wajib is refuted through the distinction between essence and individuals, and why one commentator critiques that very answer.

Core Idea
A single word can change the entire meaning in kalam definitions.

In this passage, the commentator addresses a specific objection to Sanusi's definition of al-Wajib (the Necessary Being). The objection claims a problem with the phrase 'its non-existence is not conceivable.' The commentator provides a multi-faceted refutation, but then al-Anbabi steps in to argue that the refutation itself leads to a deeper issue. This lesson walks through that debate, showing how precise wording is critical in theological definitions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the passage to identify what the objection is. It revolves around the pronoun in 'its non-existence' (عدمه) and whether it refers to the essence or individuals.
The objection is not explicitly stated but is implied; the answer clarifies it.
2
Follow the first answer
The commentator explains that the pronoun refers to individuals, not the essence. This means 'the Necessary Being' is defined as a thing whose individuals are not conceivable as non-existent.
This preserves the definition from the objection.
3
Examine the threefold refutation
The commentator then gives three independent reasons why the objection fails: (a) the definition applies to individuals, (b) the non-existence meant is external, not mental, (c) the definition is comprehensive and exclusive.
Each reason closes a different loophole.
4
Encounter al-Anbabi's critique
Al-Anbabi argues that the first answer leads to a problem: if the pronoun refers to individuals, then saying 'God's knowledge is wajib' would mean God's knowledge's individuals are not non-existent, which is incorrect. So he insists the pronoun must refer to the essence itself.
This shows the debate is not settled; it's a nuanced discussion among scholars.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Consider how subtle changes in pronoun reference affect the definition's validity and the predication of attributes to God.
This highlights the precision required in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاصل الجواب أن الضمير في قوله : (عدمه) عائد على (ما) باعتبار الأفراد، فالذي لا يصدق العقل بعدمه هو أفراد الواجب لا حقيقته، وهذا السؤال لا يرد إلا لو قال المصنف فالواجب لا يتصوّر في العقل عدمه، وهو لم يقل ذلك، وإنما عرف الواجب أن حقيقته شيء لا يتصور في العقل عدمه، فجعل عدم تصور العدم جزءاً من حقيقة الواجب هو فصلها المميز لها عن حقيقة المستحيل والجائز، ولم يحكم على تلك الحقيقة المركبة من الجنس المعبر عنه بـ (ما) والفصل المعبر عنه بقوله: (لا يتصور في العقل عدمه بأنها لا يتصور في العقل عدمها، وإنما جعل عدم تصور العدم جزءاً لها مميزاً لها عن غيرها، فلا وجه لهذا الإيراد. وأيضاً قول المصنف: (عدمه) أراد به العدم الخارجي، فلا يرد عليه ما يتصور عدمه ذهناً وإنما يرد عليه ما يتصور عدمه خارجاً. وأيضاً فمدار التعريف على أن يكون جامعاً لأفراد المعرف مانعاً من دخول غيرها، وهذا التعريف كذلك. فتحصل أن هذا الإيراد ممنوع من جهات ثلاث). وما أجاب به عنه من أن الضمير عائد على ( ما ) باعتبار الأفراد يصير عليه معنى المتن الواجب أمر لا يتصور في العقل عدم أفراده، وهذا يؤدي إلى بطلان الإخبار في قولنا: علم الله واجب؛ إذ يصير المعنى عليه : علم الله أمر لا يتصور في العقل عدم أفراده، وهذا المعنى غير صحيح فالحق أن لا إيراد ولا جواب بل الضمير عائد على (ما) باعتبار نفسها.
The summary of the answer is that the pronoun in his statement (its non-existence) refers to (what) with respect to individuals, so that whose non-existence the intellect does not affirm is the individuals of the Necessary Being (al-wajib), not its essence. This objection only arises if the author had said 'the Necessary Being is something whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect', but he did not say that. Rather, he defined the Necessary Being as that its essence is a thing whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect, making the non-conception of non-existence a part of the essence of the Necessary Being, which is its differentia (fasl) distinguishing it from the essence of the Impossible (al-mustahil) and the Contingent (al-ja'iz). He did not judge that essence—composed of the genus expressed by (what) and the differentia expressed by his saying (its non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect)—as being itself not conceivable in its non-existence. Rather, he made the non-conception of non-existence a part of it, distinguishing it from others, so there is no basis for this objection. Also, the author's word (its non-existence) intends external non-existence, so it is not vulnerable to what is conceivable as non-existent mentally, but only to what is conceivable as non-existent externally. Also, the definition is based on being comprehensive of all individuals of the defined and exclusive of others, and this definition is so. It is thus concluded that this objection is rejected from three aspects. And what he answered with—that the pronoun refers to (what) with respect to individuals—yields for the text the meaning 'the Necessary Being is an entity whose individuals are not conceivable in the intellect as non-existent', and this leads to the invalidity of the predication in our statement 'God's knowledge is necessary'; for the meaning would then become 'God's knowledge is an entity whose individuals are not conceivable in the intellect as non-existent', and this meaning is unsound. So the truth is that there is no objection and no answer, but rather the pronoun refers to (what) in itself.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection to Sanusi's definition of al-wajib (the Necessary Being). The commentator explains that the pronoun in 'its non-existence' refers to the individuals of the wajib, not the essence. He then gives three reasons why the objection fails: first, the definition only addresses the individuals, not the essence; second, the relevant non-existence is external, not mental; third, the definition is comprehensive and exclusive. Finally, al-Anbabi critiques this answer, arguing that it leads to a problematic interpretation of 'God's knowledge is necessary', and asserts that the pronoun should instead refer to the essence itself.

Key Terms
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز
العدم
الفصل
الجنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition 'something whose non-existence is not conceivable' applies directly to the essence of Allah.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that it applies only to the individuals of the wajib, not the essence itself.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer given in the passage, what does the pronoun in 'its non-existence' refer to?
A) The essence of al-wajib
B) The individuals of al-wajib
C) The contingent beings
D) The impossible beings

Key Takeaway
The definition of al-Wajib hinges on whether the pronoun refers to essence or individuals, and this subtle distinction leads to a significant scholarly debate.

What specific attribute do you struggle to accept? This lesson calls you to re-examine your understanding. Will you journal about it today?

This shows that we must be careful not to apply a general definition in a way that contradicts other truths about Allah. In your life, have you ever held a belief about Allah that, upon reflection, contradicted another truth about Him? How can you ensure your beliefs are consistent? This lesson urges you to seek coherence in your theology. What step will you take to reconcile any conflicting notions you might have about Allah?

Definition of the Impossible and Defense Against Objections
Al-Bajuri defines the impossible, answers objections about conceivability and existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise kalam definition of the impossible (مستحيل) and how it is defended against objections concerning conceivability and existence.

Core Idea
The impossible is what the mind cannot genuinely affirm to exist, not what it cannot imagine.

Al-Bajuri, commenting on Sanusi's text, provides a meticulous definition of the impossible and anticipates two key objections. This lesson unpacks his reasoning, clarifying the crucial distinction between mere imagination and mental assent. By working through the objections and answers, you will appreciate the precision required in Islamic theological terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Notice the back-and-forth of objection and answer.
The passage is dense; do not worry about details yet.
2
Understand the definition
Identify the definition: 'what is not conceived in the mind to exist.' Note the terms: conception (تصور), existence (وجود), and the role of the mind.
The definition seems straightforward, but objections arise from common misunderstandings.
3
Examine the first objection and answer
The objection: the impossible can be imagined (e.g., a partner to God). Al-Bajuri's answer: 'conception' here means 'assent' (تصديق), not mere imagination. One can imagine the impossible but cannot genuinely affirm its existence.
This clarifies why the definition uses 'conceived' in a technical sense.
4
Examine the second objection and answer
The objection: restricting to 'existence' makes the definition non-exclusive by including non-existent entities (e.g., attributes of negation). Al-Bajuri's answer: 'existence' means absolute affirmation (ثبوت). These entities are not affirmed in the mind, so they are excluded.
This shows the importance of the term 'ثبوت' in kalam.

Source Text

(قوله: مَا لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ) - بضم الياء وفتحها على ما مر .. واعتُرِضَ: بأن المستحيل قد يُتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده؛ إذ العقل قد يتصوَّرُ المحال كما تقدم. وأجيب بما مر من أن المراد بالتصور هنا: التصديق، بمعنى: الإذعان والقبول. ودخل في التعريف: كلِّ من المستحيل الضروري والنظري، فالأول: كعُرُقِ - أي: خُلُو - الجِرْمِ عن الحركة والسكون، والثاني كالشريك. وقد عرفت أن الأنواع الثلاثةَ المتقدمة تجري في المستحيل أيضاً. فتدبر. (قوله: فِي الْعَقْل) تقدَّمَ أن (أل) فيه إما للعهد أو للاستغراق، لكن بقطع النظر عن العلائق المانعة، فاندفع بذلك ما قد يقال : إنه قد يُتصوَّرُ في بعض العقول وجودُ بعض المستحيلات، فلا تَغْفَلْ (قوله: وُجُودُهُ الضمير عائد على (ما) باعتبار الأفراد نظير ما مر. ويُحِثَ في التقييد بالوجود بأنه يَصِيرُ التعريف غير مانع لدخول كل من صفات الشلُوبِ والأحوالِ فيه؛ لأنه لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل وجوده، فإنه ليس من الموجودات. وأجيب: بأن المراد بالوجود مُطلَقُ الثبوت، وحينئذ لا يَردُ ذلك؛ لأنه لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل ثبوته. فتأمل.
(His saying: 'What is not conceived') – with damm on the ya and fatha as previously mentioned. And it was objected that the impossible may be conceived in the mind to exist, for the mind can conceive the impossible as previously stated. And it was answered, as was mentioned, that the intended meaning of 'conception' here is assent, meaning acceptance and approval. And the definition includes both the necessary impossible and the theoretical impossible. The first is like the absence (i.e., vacuity) of a body from motion and rest, and the second is like the partner (to God). And you have known that the three preceding types also apply to the impossible. So reflect. (His saying: 'in the mind') – it was previously stated that the 'al' in it is either for the specific or for universal application, but disregarding the preventing relationships. Thus is repelled what might be said: that some impossibles are conceived in some minds to exist, so be heedful. (His saying: 'its existence') – the pronoun refers back to 'what' according to individuals, similar to what preceded. And it was objected in restricting to existence that the definition becomes non-exclusive, because it includes all the attributes of negations (?) and states, for their existence is not conceived in the mind, as they are not existents. And it was answered: that the intended meaning of existence is absolute affirmation; then this objection does not arise, because their affirmation is not conceived in the mind. So contemplate.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a definition and defense of the term 'impossible' (مستحيل) in kalam. Al-Bajuri begins by quoting the matn definition: 'what is not conceived in the mind to exist.' He then addresses an objection: if the impossible can be thought of (e.g., when one says 'if there were a partner to God...'), then the definition is flawed. The answer is that 'conception' here means 'assent' (تصديق), not mere imagination. One can imagine the impossible but cannot genuinely affirm it. The definition thus includes both necessary impossibles (like a body being neither moving nor at rest) and theoretical impossibles (like a partner to God). Next, Al-Bajuri clarifies 'in the mind' – it means the mind in general, ignoring individual minds that might mistakenly conceive impossibles (like Jews conceiving ‘Uzayr as son of God). Finally, he addresses an objection about restricting to 'existence': some things (like attributes of nonexistence or states) are not existents, so they would wrongly be considered impossible. The answer is that 'existence' here means absolute 'affirmation' (ثبوت), so those things are not affirmed in the mind, thus they do not enter the definition. The lesson shows the careful precision in kalam definitions.

نظري

Misconception: If something can be imagined, it cannot be impossible.
Correction: The definition uses 'conception' to mean assent (تصديق), not mere imagination. One can imagine the impossible but cannot genuinely affirm its existence.

Key Takeaway
The kalam definition of the impossible is precise: it is what the mind cannot genuinely affirm to exist, addressing objections about imagination and restricting to existence.

Objection to the Definition of al-Mustahil and Its Answer
Al-Bajuri presents an objection to the definition of al-mustahil and refutes it definitively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definition of al-mustahil (the impossible) is not undermined by the objection regarding temporal states, and how the answer hinges on distinguishing between essence and individuals, and between external and mental existence.

Core Idea
The definition of the impossible stands because it concerns external existence, not mental existence.

Al-Bajuri presents a subtle objection to the definition of the impossible and then refutes it from multiple angles. This passage sharpens our understanding of what 'existence' means in kalam definitions and why definitions must be precise. By examining the objection and its answer, you will see how careful distinctions protect theological concepts from misguided criticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a clarification about synonyms.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims the definition of al-mustahil is not comprehensive because it fails to include temporal states. Ask yourself: what are these temporal states, and why would they be problematic?
3
Follow the answer's steps
The answer makes several distinct points. List them: pronoun refers to individuals, existence means external, definitional criteria are met, and a further subtlety about 'deafness'.
The answer is attributed to al-Ajhuri.
4
Understand the 'deafness' nuance
The text raises a further problem: if the pronoun refers to individuals, then saying 'deafness is impossible for God' would be misconstrued. The correct resolution is that there is no real objection.
This shows the importance of precise pronoun reference.
5
Reflect on the three respects
The answer concludes that the objection is precluded from three respects. Identify them from the text and note how each refutes the objection.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: وَالْجَائِزُ) هو والممكنُ بمعنى واحد، فهما مترادفان.
(قوله: مَا يَصِحُ فِي الْعَقْلِ... إلخ) اعتُرِضَ: بأن هذا التعريف غيرُ جامع لعدم شُمُوله لكلّ من الأحوال والاعتبارات الحادثة؛ لأنه لا يَصِحُ في العقل وجوده وعدمه، فإنه ليس من الموجودات كما تقدم.
حاشية العلامة أحمد الأجهوري.
وحاصل الجواب: أن الضمير عائد على ( ما ) باعتبار الأفراد، فالمحكوم عليه بأنه لا يتصور في العقل وجوده أفراد المستحيل، لا حقيقته، وهذا السؤال لا يرد إلا لو قال المصنف والمستحيل: لا يتصور في العقل وجوده مع أنه لم يقل ذلك، وإنما عرفه وبين أن حقيقته شيء لا يتصور وجوده، فجعل عدم تصور الوجود جزءاً من حقيقته مميزاً لها عن حقيقة الواجب والجائز، ولم يحكم على هذه الحقيقة المركبة من الجنس المعبر عنه بـ (ما) والفصل الذي هو عدم تصور الوجود بأنها لا يتصور وجودها، وإنما جعل عدم تصور الوجود فصلاً لها مميزاً لها عن حقيقة الواجب والجائز، فلا وجه لهذا الإيراد. وأيضاً الوجود المذكور في التعريف المراد به الوجود الخارجي لا الذهني، فلا يرد على التعريف إلا ما يتصور في العقل وجوده خارجاً، وحقيقة المستحيل لا يتصور في العقل إلا وجودها ذهناً، وأيضاً فمدار التعريف على أن يكون جامعاً لأفراد المعرف مانعاً من دخول غيرها، وهذا التعريف كذلك. فتحصل أن هذا الإيراد ممنوع من جهات ثلاث. وما أجاب به عنه من أن الضمير عائد على ( ما ) باعتبار الأفراد يصير المعنى عليه: أن المستحيل أمر لا يتصور في العقل وجود أفراده، وهذا المعنى يؤدي إلى بطلان الإخبار في قولنا: الصمم مستحيل على الله؛ إذ يصير المعنى عليه الصمم أمر لا يتصور في العقل وجود أفراده، مع أن المقصود : الحكم عليه بأنه لا يتصور وجوده. فالحق أن لا إيراد ولا جواب والضمير عائد على (ما) باعتبار نفسها.
(His statement: 'and al-ja'iz') it and al-mumkin are one in meaning, so they are synonymous. (His statement: 'what is valid in the intellect... etc.) It was objected: that this definition is not comprehensive due to its not including all of the temporal states and considerations; because their existence and non-existence are not valid in the intellect, since they are not among existents as previously mentioned. Commentary of al-Allama Ahmad al-Ajhuri. The sum of the answer: that the pronoun refers to 'ma' (what) with consideration of individuals, so the thing judged that its existence is not conceivable in the intellect is the individuals of the impossible, not its essence. And this question does not arise unless the author had said 'and the impossible: its existence is not conceivable in the intellect,' but he did not say that. Rather, he defined it and explained that its essence is something whose existence is not conceivable, so he made inconceivability of existence part of its essence distinguishing it from the essence of the necessary and the possible, and he did not judge that this essence—composed of the genus expressed by 'ma' and the differentia which is inconceivability of existence—is such that its existence is not conceivable. Rather, he made inconceivability of existence a differentia for it distinguishing it from the essence of the necessary and the possible, so there is no basis for this objection. Also, the existence mentioned in the definition is intended to mean external existence, not mental existence; so nothing applies to the definition except what is conceivable in the intellect as existing externally, and the essence of the impossible is only conceivable in the intellect as mentally existent. Also, the basis of a definition is that it be comprehensive of all individuals of the definiendum and exclusive of others, and this definition is so. It follows that this objection is precluded from three respects. And what he answered with—that the pronoun refers to 'ma' with consideration of individuals—makes the meaning: that the impossible is a thing whose individuals' existence is not conceivable in the intellect. This meaning leads to the invalidity of the statement in our saying 'deafness is impossible for God,' because the meaning becomes 'deafness is a thing whose individuals' existence is not conceivable in the intellect,' whereas the intended meaning is to judge that its existence is not conceivable. So the truth is that there is no objection and no answer, and the pronoun refers to 'ma' in respect of itself.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a classic kalam objection-and-answer about the definition of al-mustahil (the impossible). Al-Bajuri first clarifies that al-ja'iz (the possible) and al-mumkin (the contingent) are synonyms. Then he reports an objection: the definition of al-mustahil as 'what is not valid in the intellect to exist' fails to include temporal states (ahwal) and considerations (i'tibarat) because they are not actual existents, so their existence and non-existence are not valid in the intellect. The answer, attributed to al-Ajhuri, unfolds in several steps. First, the pronoun in 'its existence' refers to the individuals of the impossible, not its essence; the definition is about the individuals, so it is comprehensive. Second, the objection only works if the author had said 'the impossible: its existence is not conceivable,' but he actually defined its essence as something whose existence is not conceivable, making inconceivability a differentia. Third, 'existence' in the definition means external existence, not mental existence; the impossible essence is conceivable mentally, but not externally, so the definition holds. Fourth, definitional criteria of comprehensiveness and exclusivity are met. A further subtle point is raised: if the pronoun refers to individuals, then saying 'deafness is impossible for God' would mean 'deafness is something whose individuals are inconceivable,' which is not the intended meaning. The correct position is that there is no genuine objection; the pronoun refers to 'ma' itself, and the definition stands as given.

الممكن (al-mumkin)
المستحيل (al-mustahil)
الوجود الخارجي (al-wujud al-khariji)
الوجود الذهني (al-wujud al-dhihni)
الأفراد (afrad)
الحقيقة (haqiqa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of al-mustahil fails because it doesn't include mentally existent things.
Correction: The definition only concerns external existence; the impossible is not externally existent, so the definition is correct.

Checkpoint

A) Both external and mental existence
B) External existence only

Key Takeaway
The definition of al-mustahil is sound because it refers to external existence, and the objection confuses mental and external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an objection to a core belief, do you immediately seek a complex answer, or do you first examine whether the objection is even valid? How many times have you let a clever-sounding doubt unsettle your faith without checking its foundations?
?
This lesson shows that a single misdirected pronoun can change the entire meaning. Are you careful with the words you use when describing Allah? Do you distinguish between what is true of Him externally and what is merely a mental conception?
?
The truth is that there is no objection and no answer. How often do you complicate your faith by entertaining doubts that have no real basis? Will you commit to testing every doubt against the precise definitions of the scholars before accepting it?

Understanding al-Haditha: Temporal States and Considerations
Al-Almani defines 'al-haditha' and distinguishes temporal states from considerations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'al-haditha' as it applies to both temporal states (ahwal) and considerations (i'tibarat) in kalam.

Core Idea
Temporal things include both necessary states and accidental considerations.

In this passage, the commentator Al-Almani clarifies the term 'al-haditha' (the temporal) used in the earlier objection. He distinguishes between two types: al-ahwal (states) which are necessary concomitants of a substance, and al-i'tibarat (considerations) which are accidental attributes subsisting in a subject. This distinction is crucial for understanding the rational judgments about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get the overall meaning.
Focus on the two categories introduced.
2
Identify the two categories
Notice that Al-Almani divides temporal things into al-ahwal (states) and al-i'tibarat (considerations). State the difference in your own words.
States are necessary concomitants; considerations are accidental attributes.
3
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: Zayd being white (state), Zayd being knowing (state), subsistence of knowledge in Zayd (consideration). Understand how each fits its category.
Also note the contrast with God's power being an eternal consideration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قوله: (الْحَادِثَةِ صفةٌ لكل من الأحوال والاعتبارات، فالأحوال الحادثة ككون زيد أبيض اللازم لبياضه، وكون زيد عالماً اللازم لعلمه ومثال الاعتبارات الحادثة: قيام العلم بزيد، فإنه أمر اعتباري حادث، كما أن قيام القدرة بذات الله أمر اعتباري إلا أنه قديم. تقريرات العلامة محمد الألماني (قوله: لِكُلِّ مِنَ الْأَحْوَالِ وَالْاِعْتِبَارَاتِ الْحَادِثَةِ ككون زيد قائماً، وكصحة زيد. وسيأتي الكلام على الأحوال والاعتبارات.
(His statement: 'al-haditha' is an attribute for both al-ahwal (states) and al-i'tibarat (considerations). So al-ahwal al-haditha (temporal states) are like Zayd being white, which is necessary for his whiteness, and Zayd being knowing, necessary for his knowledge. An example of temporal considerations is the subsistence of knowledge in Zayd, for it is a temporal consideration, just as the subsistence of power in the essence of God is a consideration but eternal. Commentaries of al-Allama Muhammad al-Almani: (His statement: 'for each of the temporal states and considerations, such as Zayd standing and Zayd's health.' And later discussion will come on al-ahwal and al-i'tibarat.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a brief lexical and conceptual clarification. Al-Almani explains that the term 'al-haditha' (the temporal) in the earlier objection qualifies both al-ahwal (states) and al-i'tibarat (considerations). He defines each: temporal states are necessary concomitants (lazim) of a substance, like the whiteness of Zayd entails the state 'Zayd being white'; similarly, knowledge entails 'Zayd being knowing'. Temporal considerations are accidental attributes that subsist in a subject, like 'the subsistence of knowledge in Zayd' (a mental reality that is temporal) as opposed to 'the subsistence of power in God's essence', which is a consideration but eternal. The second part reiterates that the phrase 'for each of the temporal states and considerations' includes examples like Zayd standing and Zayd's health. The note ends with a forward reference to a later discussion on this topic.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think states and considerations are the same thing.
Correction: States are necessary concomitants (e.g., Zayd being white is necessary for his whiteness), while considerations are accidental attributes subsisting in a subject (e.g., the subsistence of knowledge in Zayd is a temporal consideration).

Al-Ja'iz: Necessary vs. Theoretical Possibility
Al-Sanusi divides al-ja'iz into necessary and theoretical with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between necessary possible (al-ja'iz al-daruri) and theoretical possible (al-ja'iz al-nazari), and why some rational possibilities require theological reflection.

Core Idea
What is possible by reason may still require divine revelation to be actual.

Al-Sanusi now refines the category of al-ja'iz by dividing it into two subtypes: that which the intellect grasps immediately, and that which requires deeper reasoning. This distinction is crucial for understanding how rational possibility relates to divine action. The passage also provides concrete examples that distinguish what is rationally possible from what is legally (shar') possible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of al-ja'iz
The passage states that al-ja'iz includes both necessary and theoretical. First grasp what 'necessary possible' means (self-evident) vs. 'theoretical possible' (requires proof).
2
Examine the examples
Look at the examples: motion/rest for necessary, punishing the obedient/rewarding the disobedient for theoretical. Note the distinction between rational and legal possibility.
The example of punishing the obedient is rationally possible but legally impossible.
3
Reflect on why it's theoretical
The passage explains that these matters require reflection on the proof of God's oneness and that He is the sole creator. Without that, the intellect initially assumes reward/punishment are necessary.
This counters the Mu'tazilite view.
4
Consider the implication about injustice
Note the definition of injustice and how it does not apply to God. This reinforces the Ash'ari position on divine sovereignty.
